Baqillani's Defense of Uthman Against the Letter Incident
Baqillani refutes the letter incident accusation against Uthman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's rational refutation of the accusation that Uthman wrote a seditious letter, and the principle that a Muslim's oath must be trusted.

Core Idea
Uthman's oath trumps an unproven accusation.

This passage defends Uthman against a historical accusation that he wrote a letter ordering harm to certain Companions. Baqillani argues that Uthman swore an oath denying the letter, and that every Muslim is obliged to believe his oath. He also refutes the demand to hand over Marwan, the alleged writer. Let's examine Baqillani's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the structure: Baqillani presents several grounds ('وجوه') for rejecting the accusation.
The final part of the Arabic is garbled; focus on the clear arguments.
2
Identify the first ground
Notice that Baqillani begins with Uthman's acknowledgment of the slave and camel, followed by his oath that he did not write the letter. This is the first argument.
Uthman's oath is presented as a strong proof.
3
Understand the second ground
Baqillani states that it is the right of every Muslim to trust Uthman's oath. This establishes a general principle of credibility.
This principle applies to any Muslim, especially a Companion.
4
Analyze the demand for Marwan
The opponents demanded that Uthman hand over Marwan, alleging he wrote the letter. Baqillani refutes this because Marwan was with Uthman and denied writing it.
The garbled final sentence likely concludes that no action could be taken.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما تعلقوا به من أمر الكتاب الذي وجدوه مع عبده على بعيره وما تضمنه في بابهم وباب محمد بن أبي بكر مما أمر به عبد الله بن أبي سرح ، فلا حجة عليه من وجوه أحدها أنه اعترف أن العبد عبده والبعير بعيره وحلف لهم أنه ما كتب الكتاب ولا أمر من كتبه. وقد كان من حق كل مسلم سمع قَسَمَ عثمان أن يصدقه ويثق بقوله، فضلاً عن يمينه وأما افتياتهم عليـه بعد ذلك وقولهم : تُسلِمُ مروان لأنه هو كاتبه فإنه مطالبة بمعصية لأنه سأل وهو يومئذ معه في الدار فأنكر أن يكون كتبه فلم يجز لـه ذلك مروان عن من الذهب وهو من الأثاث
As for their clinging to the matter of the letter which they found with his slave on his camel, and what it contained regarding their case and the case of Muhammad ibn Abi Bakr, which Abdullah ibn Abi Sarh ordered, there is no argument against him on several grounds. One of them is that he acknowledged that the slave was his slave and the camel his camel, and he swore to them that he did not write the letter nor order anyone to write it. And it was the right of every Muslim who heard Uthman's oath to believe him and trust his word, let alone his oath. As for their impertinence against him after that and their saying: 'Hand over Marwan because he is the one who wrote it,' it is a demand for disobedience, because he asked while Marwan at that time was with him in the house, and he denied having written it, so it was not permissible for him [to hand him over]? Marwan from? gold? and it is from the furnishings?
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage defends Uthman against the accusation that he wrote a letter to Abdullah ibn Abi Sarh ordering harm against certain Companions. Baqillani gives several points: First, Uthman admitted the slave and camel were his but swore he neither wrote nor ordered the letter. Second, every Muslim is obliged to believe Uthman's oath. Third, the demand to hand over Marwan (alleged writer) is invalid because Marwan was present with Uthman and denied writing it. The final part is garbled but likely concludes that no action could be taken.

Key Terms
الكتاب (the letter)
قسم عثمان (Uthman's oath)
مروان (Marwan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The letter found with Uthman's slave proves that Uthman conspired against certain Companions.
Correction: Baqillani shows that Uthman swore an oath denying the letter, and his oath must be believed. Additionally, the alleged writer Marwan denied involvement.

Checkpoint
What was one reason Baqillani gives for rejecting the accusation about the letter?
A) The letter was lost
B) Uthman swore he did not write it, and his oath must be believed
C) The letter was written by Marwan without Uthman's knowledge
D) The slave confessed to forging it

Key Takeaway
A Muslim's oath, especially of a companion like Uthman, should be trusted over unsubstantiated accusations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you been quick to judge or believe an accusation against someone without giving them a chance to defend themselves with their word? Will you commit to trusting the oath of a fellow Muslim as the Companions did with Uthman?
?
Uthman's oath was enough for the believers, but do you often demand excessive proof from those you already know to be righteous? How can you train yourself to take their word at face value?
?
The demand to hand over Marwan was based on mere suspicion. Have you ever insisted on punishing someone based on unsubstantiated claims? What holds you back from requiring clear evidence before acting?

Defense of Capable Governors
Baqillani defends Uthman's appointment of capable governors against criticism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's defense of Uthman's appointment of capable governors, addressing the criticism that they lacked asceticism.

Core Idea
Capability in governance, not asceticism, is the criterion for appointing governors.

Imam Baqillani responds to a common criticism against Uthman ibn Affan regarding his appointment of certain governors, notably Muawiyah. He argues that these appointees possessed the necessary qualities for leadership and points out that the same person served as governor under Umar without objection. This lesson examines the logic and consistency of Baqillani's defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the concise argument: the appointees were 'people of bravery, sufficiency, insight, and ability' even if not ascetics.
2
Identify the key argument
Baqillani establishes that the criterion for appointment is capability (bravery, insight, ability), not asceticism. This directly counters the criticism that Uthman appointed non-ascetics.
The term 'النفر' refers to the specific individuals criticized.
3
Consider the consistency argument
Baqillani points out that Muawiyah was appointed by Umar and served his entire tenure without objection. This highlights inconsistency in the critics, as they condemned Uthman for the same action that they accepted from Umar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الولاية وقد كان هؤلاء النفر أهل نجدة وكفاية وبصَر بالإمرة وقدرة عليها وإن لم يكونوا زهاداً وقد كان معاوية من أمراء عمر طول مدته فمـا نقـم عليـه أحد .
As for the governorship, these individuals were people of bravery, sufficiency, insight into command, and ability for it, even if they were not ascetics. Muawiyah was among the governors of Umar throughout his entire tenure, and no one objected to him.
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage is a direct refutation of a criticism that Uthman appointed governors who were not ascetics. The author states that these individuals possessed the necessary qualities for leadership (bravery, sufficiency, insight, ability). He then points out that Muawiyah, one of the criticized appointees, was appointed by Umar and served for his entire caliphate without any objection. This implies that the same people who criticized Uthman did not object to Umar's appointment, thus the criticism is inconsistent.

Key Terms
الولاية
النفر
معاوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Uthman appointed incompetent or unqualified governors, particularly those lacking religious piety.
Correction: Baqillani argues that the appointees were capable (brave, insightful, able) even if not ascetics, and notes that Muawiyah served as governor under Umar without objection, showing the criticism is inconsistent.

Checkpoint
What point does Baqillani make about Muawiyah?
A) Muawiyah was a close relative of Uthman.
B) Muawiyah was appointed by Umar and no one objected.
C) Muawiyah was an ascetic.
D) Muawiyah was a bad governor.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani defends Uthman's appointments by highlighting the appointees' capability and noting that Muawiyah served under Umar without objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Recall a time you judged a leader or decision based on outward piety rather than competence. How does Baqillani's defense of capable governors challenge your assumption? Will you commit to reassessing your criteria for leadership starting today?
?
Muawiyah served under Umar without objection, yet under Uthman he was criticized. In your own life, do you hold the same standards for everyone, or do you unconsciously apply double standards? What would it take for you to be consistent like Baqillani shows?
?
If you were entrusted with appointing a leader in your community, would you prioritize capability or religious image? This lesson argues for capability. How does your answer align with the lesson? What needs to change?

Defending Uthman Against the Khums Accusation
Baqillani refutes the claim that Uthman gave the khums of Ifriqiyya to Marwan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the accusation that Uthman misused the khums of Ifriqiyya and the principle that accusations must be proven.

Core Idea
Unproven accusations against the Companions are not permissible.

Baqillani addresses a sectarian claim that Uthman gave the entire khums of Ifriqiyya to his relative Marwan. He firmly declares it false, highlights Uthman's piety and generosity, and dismisses a weak excuse that Uthman borrowed from the treasury. This lesson walks through his refutation and the underlying principle of requiring proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice how Baqillani begins by directly stating the accusation and then refuting it.
The passage contains both the accusation and the refutation in a single argument.
2
Identify the accusation and the refutation
Pick out the specific claim (giving the khums to Marwan) and Baqillani's three points: the claim is false, Uthman is too pious, and the borrowing excuse is weak.
3
Understand the principle of proof
The final sentence establishes a key principle: as long as the accusation is not proven, it is not permissible to accuse Uthman. Reflect on why this matters in defending the Companions.
This principle safeguards the honor of the Sahaba.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قولهم إنه كان يحبهم ويخصهم بالعطاء وإنه أعطى مروان جميع خُمس إفريقية فإنه باطل وتوهم منهم وقد كان عثمان أتقى الله وأنْزَة نفساً مع إنفاقه في سبيل الله وكثرة بذله لماله ونفسه في نُصرَةِ الدين. وقد ذُكر أنه إنما أعطى من مال نفسه وقال لهم مرة في قسمه : «إنما اقترضت من بيت مال المسلمين؛ وهذا ضعيف؛ لأنه كان غير محتاج إلى اقتراض مع سعة ماله ؛ ومتى لم يثبت عليه ذلك، لم يحل قرفه به.
As for their statement that he loved them and singled them out with gifts, and that he gave Marwan all of the khums of Ifriqiyya, it is false and a delusion on their part. Uthman was more God-fearing and had a more dignified soul, despite his spending in the way of God and his abundant sacrifice of his wealth and himself in support of religion. It has been mentioned that he only gave from his own wealth, and he said to them once in his division: 'I only borrowed from the treasury of the Muslims.' This is weak, because he was not in need of borrowing given the abundance of his wealth. As long as that is not proven against him, it is not permissible to accuse him of it.
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage refutes the accusation that Uthman gave the entire khums of Ifriqiyya to Marwan. The author declares the claim false and a delusion. He then highlights Uthman's piety and generosity. He addresses the weak excuse that Uthman borrowed from the treasury, arguing it is implausible given Uthman's wealth. The final line establishes a principle: as long as the accusation is not proven, it is not permissible to accuse him.

Key Terms
خمس إفريقية
مروان
بيت مال المسلمين
القرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that Uthman misused public funds by giving the khums to Marwan and that he borrowed from the treasury to cover it.
Correction: Baqillani shows the accusation is false, Uthman was pious, and the borrowing excuse is weak because he was wealthy. The principle is that without proof, such accusations are impermissible.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why is the excuse that Uthman borrowed from the treasury weak?
A) Because Uthman was poor.
B) Because Uthman was not in need of borrowing given his wealth.
C) Because he had already repaid the loan.
D) Because it was not mentioned in historical records.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani defends Uthman by dismissing the accusation as false and emphasizing that unproven charges against a Companion are not permissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever believed or repeated an accusation against someone you considered righteous without solid evidence? What would it cost you to follow the principle of this lesson and stop yourself before speaking?
?
Uthman was generous and God-conscious. Think of a person in your community who is known for piety but has been wrongly criticized. Is there a step you can take this week to defend their honor or set the record straight?
?
The lesson warns that accusing without proof is not permissible. Where in your own heart do you hold suspicions against others that are not based on proven facts? What will you do to release those suspicions and replace them with good assumptions?

Defending Uthman's Disciplinary Authority
Baqillani defends Uthman's use of a stick in disciplining Companions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that the imam has discretionary authority in disciplining, and that Uthman's use of a stick was within permissible bounds.

Core Idea
The imam’s authority includes choosing the appropriate punishment, even up to a thousand strikes with a stick.

This passage addresses a criticism leveled against Caliph Uthman for using a stick rather than a whip when disciplining some Companions. Baqillani defends Uthman by asserting the imam's full authority over corrective punishment and by citing that even Umar would have done the same if needed. Let's examine the reasoning and its implications for understanding the scope of imamic authority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: beginning with the criticism, then the defense, then the comparison with Umar.
Pay attention to the words 'لا عتب عليه' – 'no blame upon him' – as the author's firm stance.
2
Identify the criticism and response
The criticism is that Uthman exceeded using a whip to using a stick. Baqillani responds by stating the imam's authority over correction, listing permissible methods from whip to stick to reprimand.
The list includes a range from one to a thousand strokes with a stick.
3
Understand the principle of imamic authority
Baqillani establishes that the imam (leader) has the right of 'taqwim' (correction) and discretion in the type and severity of punishment. This is key to the defense.
This principle underpins the entire argument.
4
Analyze the comparison with Umar
Baqillani concludes that even Umar, known for his firmness, would have used the stick if necessary. This shows Uthman's action was not an innovation but consistent with precedents.
Think about why citing Umar strengthens the argument.
5
Reflect on the broader implications
Consider how this defense sets a precedent for imamic authority in discipline. How does this passage relate to the concept of leadership in Islam?
This connects to larger discussions in Islamic political thought.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تعلقهم بما أنكرته عائشة وغيرها من تجاوزه في تأديب الصحابة الضربَ بالدّرِّةِ إلى الضرب بالعصا فلا عتب عليه في ذلك لأن للإمام التقويم والضرب بالدّرّةِ والضرب من الواحد إلى الألف مرة بالعصا ومرة بالدرة ومرة بالانتهار والقول ولو أن عمر احتاج معهم إلى العصا لفعل.
As for their clinging to what Aisha and others disapproved of, regarding his exceeding in disciplining the Companions from striking with the whip to striking with the stick, there is no blame upon him in that, because the imam has the authority of correction and striking with the whip, and striking from one to a thousand times with the stick, and once with the whip, and once with reprimand and speech. Even if Umar had needed the stick with them, he would have done so.
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage defends Uthman against the criticism that he escalated punishment from using a whip to using a stick. The author argues that the imam has full authority over correction, including the type and severity of punishment—from a single whip stroke to up to a thousand strikes with a stick, or even just a verbal reprimand. He then points out that Umar, if he had found it necessary, would have used the stick as well, implying that Uthman acted within permissible bounds.

Key Terms
الضرب بالدرة
الضرب بالعصا
الإمام
التقويم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Uthman acted harshly and beyond his authority by using a stick instead of a whip.
Correction: Baqillani argues the imam has full authority to choose the method of correction, including the stick, and that Umar would have done the same if needed.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument in defense of Uthman's use of the stick?
A) Uthman was wrong but forgivable.
B) The imam has full authority to decide punishment, and Umar would have done the same.
C) The Companions deserved it.
D) Aisha was mistaken.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani defends Uthman by asserting the imam's broad authority in disciplinary matters, including the use of a stick, and citing Umar's hypothetical similar action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a leader making a harsh decision, do you quickly assume injustice? How does this passage challenge your instant judgment and require you to consider their authority and context?
?
Have you ever rejected someone's authority because you disagreed with their method? What would it mean for you to trust the imam's discretion even when it seems severe, as the Companions were expected to trust Uthman?
?
If you were in a position of authority, would you have the courage to discipline as needed, knowing some would disapprove? What holds you back from using the full extent of your responsibility, and how does this passage push you to reconsider?

Uthman's Oath and the Illegitimate Demand
Baqillani presents two arguments: Uthman's oath and the illegality of demanding Marwan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that Uthman's sworn denial must be accepted and that the rebels' demand to hand over Marwan was unlawful.

Core Idea
Uthman's oath suffices; the rebels had no right to demand Marwan.

Baqillani begins his defense of Uthman by addressing the infamous letter accusation. He first establishes that Uthman swore he did not write it, which every Muslim must accept. He then rebuts the demand that Marwan be handed over, showing it was both unjust and illegal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Uthman's oath
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Pay attention to Uthman's admission of ownership but denial of writing the letter.
2
Understand the first evidence
Baqillani's first argument: Uthman swore he did not write or order the letter. Every Muslim must trust his oath.
This is based on the principle of accepting a Muslim's oath.
3
Analyze the demand for Marwan
The rebels demanded Marwan be handed over as the alleged writer. Baqillani calls this a 'demand for sin' because Marwan denied writing it and handing him over would lead to vigilante justice.
The text has a garbled section here, but the overall meaning is clear.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why Baqillani emphasizes that the demand is sinful. It shows that the rebels overstepped their authority and acted unjustly.
This connects to the broader theme of rejecting rebel claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما تعلقوا به من أمر الكتاب الذي وجدوه مع عبده على بعيره وما تضمنه في بابهم وباب محمد بن أبي بكر مما أمر به عبد الله بن أبي سرح ، فلا حجة عليه من وجوه أحدها أنه اعترف أن العبد عبده والبعير بعيره وحلف لهم أنه ما كتب الكتاب ولا أمر من كتبه. وقد كان من حق كل مسلم سمع قَسَمَ عثمان أن يصدقه ويثق بقوله، فضلاً عن يمينه وأما افتياتهم عليـه بعد ذلك وقولهم : تُسلِمُ مروان لأنه هو كاتبه فإنه مطالبة بمعصية لأنه سأل وهو يومئذ معه في الدار فأنكر أن يكون كتبه فلم يجز لـه ذلك مروان عن من الذهب وهو من الأثاث [؟] تسليمه إليهم ليتحكموا فيه فلو أنه أيضاً اعترف بالكتاب لم يحلّ دمه ولو حل أيضاً دمه لم تكن إقامة الحد لهم ولم يجز لإمام المسلمين أن يمكنهم من إقامة الحدود وهم رعاع ليس إليهم هذا الشأن ولا هم بمأمونين عليه. وعلى أنه لو ثبت أن عثمان ومروان كتبا الكتاب لم يكن ذلك بذنب لهما لأن أولئك القوم كانوا مستحقين له لسعيهم على عثمان وحصرهم له واستنفار الناس عليه وشتمه وتحصيبه على منبر رسول الله صلّى الله عليه وسلم ومنعه الـمـاء واستخفافهم بسلطان الله وحصرهم الصحابة في منازلهم وتقدم الغافقي على سائرهم واستيلائهم على المدينة وبدون هذه الأفعال يُكتب بما كتب به عثمان فليت القوم ،انصرفوا وليت الكتاب وصل فإنه لم يكن والله أعلم يجري ما جرى من قتل عثمان وما أثمر من سفك الدماء بعده وما نحن في بقيته إلى اليوم فإن قتل أولئك كان من الصلاح في الدين وتمكينهم ما حاولوه من أعظم الفساد وقد أعقب من الشّتات والفرقة وسوء العاقبة ما لا ينقطع في غالب الظن إلى يوم القيامة.
As for their clinging to the matter of the letter that they found with his slave on his camel, and what it contained regarding them and regarding Muhammad ibn Abi Bakr, which he had ordered Abdullah ibn Abi Sarh to do, there is no argument against him from several aspects. One of them is that he admitted that the slave was his slave and the camel was his camel, and he swore to them that he did not write the letter nor did he order anyone to write it. It was the right of every Muslim who heard Uthman's oath to believe him and trust his word, let alone his oath. As for their high-handedness against him after that, and their saying: 'Hand over Marwan because he is its writer,' it is a demand for sin because they asked while he (Marwan) was with him in the house, and he denied having written it, so that was not permissible for him. And Marwan... [unclear] ... handing him over to them so they could take control of him. Even if he had admitted to the letter, his blood would not have been lawful. And even if his blood had been lawful, the execution of the prescribed punishment would not have been for them, and it is not permissible for the Imam of the Muslims to allow them to carry out the punishments, for they are a rabble, not having authority over this matter, nor are they trustworthy regarding it. Moreover, even if it were established that Uthman and Marwan wrote the letter, that would not have been a sin for them, because those people deserved it for their efforts against Uthman, their besieging him, mobilizing people against him, insulting him, pelting him with stones from the pulpit of the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, preventing him from water, belittling the authority of God, confining the Companions in their homes, the advancement of al-Ghafiqi over them, and their seizure of Madinah. Without these actions, the letter would not have been written as Uthman wrote it. If only the people had refrained, if only the letter had reached, for (by God, I swear) what happened—the killing of Uthman and the subsequent bloodshed that we are still suffering from today—would not have occurred. The killing of those people was for the good of religion, while enabling what they attempted was the greatest corruption. It has resulted in such fragmentation, division, and evil consequences that, in all probability, will not cease until the Day of Judgment.
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage is a multifaceted refutation of the accusation that Uthman wrote a letter ordering the killing of certain Companions. The author presents several independent arguments (wujooh): 

1. First, Uthman admitted ownership of the slave and camel but swore he did not write or order the letter. Every Muslim must accept his oath. 
2. Second, demanding the handover of Marwan (the alleged writer) is a sinful demand because Marwan denied writing it, and handing him over would lead to vigilante justice. 
3. Third, even if Uthman had admitted writing the letter, his blood would not be lawful (rebellion against a rightful imam is not justified by such an act). 
4. Fourth, even if his blood were lawful, the rebels had no authority to carry out punishment; only the imam can enforce penalties. 
5. Fifth, even if the letter were genuine, Uthman and Marwan would be blameless because the recipients deserved it due to their rebellion, siege, insults, and other crimes. The letter was a response to their aggression. 
6. Finally, the author laments that if the letter had reached its destination, the disastrous killing of Uthman and subsequent bloodshed might have been prevented, whereas allowing the rebels to proceed caused immense and lasting corruption.

Key Terms
الكتاب
العبد
البعير
القسم
مروان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The letter found with Uthman's slave proves Uthman's guilt and justifies his murder.
Correction: Baqillani argues that Uthman's oath must be trusted, and even if the letter were genuine, the rebels had no authority to punish him.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument Baqillani makes regarding the letter?
A) Uthman admitted the slave and camel were his but swore he did not write the letter.
B) Uthman confessed to writing the letter.
C) The letter was a forgery by the rebels.
D) Marwan admitted to writing the letter.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani argues that Uthman's oath must be trusted and the rebels' demand to hand over Marwan was an unlawful sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been quick to accuse someone based on circumstantial evidence, like the rebels did with Uthman? In light of this lesson, what does your heart say about trusting a fellow Muslim's oath? Will you commit to giving the benefit of the doubt before passing judgment?
?
When you want something from someone, even if you think it is justified, do you ever demand it in a way that is sinful? This lesson calls the rebels' demand a 'demand for sin.' Where in your life might you be demanding something that violates Islamic principles? What will you do to change that?

The Justified Letter and the Consequences of Rebellion
Baqillani argues that even if the letter was genuine, it was justified and the rebels' actions led to disaster.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that even if Uthman wrote the letter, the rebels deserved it and his killing was unjustified, and the long-term harm of their rebellion.

Core Idea
Even a genuine letter would not justify Uthman's murder; the rebels' sins were far greater.

Baqillani continues his defense by considering the worst-case scenario: even if Uthman and Marwan wrote the letter, it would not have been a sin because the rebels' crimes—siege, insults, usurpation—warranted punishment. He laments the tragic outcome that followed Uthman's killing, which led to endless bloodshed and division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the hypothetical genuine letter
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the conditional statements: 'even if he admitted...' and 'even if it were established...'.
2
Identify the three sub-arguments
Baqillani makes three points: 1) Even if Uthman admitted the letter, his blood was not lawful. 2) Even if his blood were lawful, the rebels had no authority to punish. 3) Even if the letter were genuine, the rebels deserved it due to their crimes.
These are nested conditional arguments.
3
Review the list of rebel crimes
Baqillani enumerates the rebels' offenses: besieging, insulting, preventing water, confining Companions, etc. These justified the letter.
The list is detailed to show their extreme aggression.
4
Reflect on the lament
Baqillani wishes the letter had reached so Uthman's killing and subsequent bloodshed would have been avoided. He calls the rebels' actions 'the greatest corruption'.
This shows the tragic consequences of their rebellion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما تعلقوا به من أمر الكتاب الذي وجدوه مع عبده على بعيره وما تضمنه في بابهم وباب محمد بن أبي بكر مما أمر به عبد الله بن أبي سرح ، فلا حجة عليه من وجوه أحدها أنه اعترف أن العبد عبده والبعير بعيره وحلف لهم أنه ما كتب الكتاب ولا أمر من كتبه. وقد كان من حق كل مسلم سمع قَسَمَ عثمان أن يصدقه ويثق بقوله، فضلاً عن يمينه وأما افتياتهم عليـه بعد ذلك وقولهم : تُسلِمُ مروان لأنه هو كاتبه فإنه مطالبة بمعصية لأنه سأل وهو يومئذ معه في الدار فأنكر أن يكون كتبه فلم يجز لـه ذلك مروان عن من الذهب وهو من الأثاث [؟] تسليمه إليهم ليتحكموا فيه فلو أنه أيضاً اعترف بالكتاب لم يحلّ دمه ولو حل أيضاً دمه لم تكن إقامة الحد لهم ولم يجز لإمام المسلمين أن يمكنهم من إقامة الحدود وهم رعاع ليس إليهم هذا الشأن ولا هم بمأمونين عليه. وعلى أنه لو ثبت أن عثمان ومروان كتبا الكتاب لم يكن ذلك بذنب لهما لأن أولئك القوم كانوا مستحقين له لسعيهم على عثمان وحصرهم له واستنفار الناس عليه وشتمه وتحصيبه على منبر رسول الله صلّى الله عليه وسلم ومنعه الـمـاء واستخفافهم بسلطان الله وحصرهم الصحابة في منازلهم وتقدم الغافقي على سائرهم واستيلائهم على المدينة وبدون هذه الأفعال يُكتب بما كتب به عثمان فليت القوم ،انصرفوا وليت الكتاب وصل فإنه لم يكن والله أعلم يجري ما جرى من قتل عثمان وما أثمر من سفك الدماء بعده وما نحن في بقيته إلى اليوم فإن قتل أولئك كان من الصلاح في الدين وتمكينهم ما حاولوه من أعظم الفساد وقد أعقب من الشّتات والفرقة وسوء العاقبة ما لا ينقطع في غالب الظن إلى يوم القيامة.
As for their clinging to the matter of the letter that they found with his slave on his camel, and what it contained regarding them and regarding Muhammad ibn Abi Bakr, which he had ordered Abdullah ibn Abi Sarh to do, there is no argument against him from several aspects. One of them is that he admitted that the slave was his slave and the camel was his camel, and he swore to them that he did not write the letter nor did he order anyone to write it. It was the right of every Muslim who heard Uthman's oath to believe him and trust his word, let alone his oath. As for their high-handedness against him after that, and their saying: 'Hand over Marwan because he is its writer,' it is a demand for sin because they asked while he (Marwan) was with him in the house, and he denied having written it, so that was not permissible for him. And Marwan... [unclear] ... handing him over to them so they could take control of him. Even if he had admitted to the letter, his blood would not have been lawful. And even if his blood had been lawful, the execution of the prescribed punishment would not have been for them, and it is not permissible for the Imam of the Muslims to allow them to carry out the punishments, for they are a rabble, not having authority over this matter, nor are they trustworthy regarding it. Moreover, even if it were established that Uthman and Marwan wrote the letter, that would not have been a sin for them, because those people deserved it for their efforts against Uthman, their besieging him, mobilizing people against him, insulting him, pelting him with stones from the pulpit of the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, preventing him from water, belittling the authority of God, confining the Companions in their homes, the advancement of al-Ghafiqi over them, and their seizure of Madinah. Without these actions, the letter would not have been written as Uthman wrote it. If only the people had refrained, if only the letter had reached, for (by God, I swear) what happened—the killing of Uthman and the subsequent bloodshed that we are still suffering from today—would not have occurred. The killing of those people was for the good of religion, while enabling what they attempted was the greatest corruption. It has resulted in such fragmentation, division, and evil consequences that, in all probability, will not cease until the Day of Judgment.
Source pages p. 543

Commentary
This passage is a multifaceted refutation of the accusation that Uthman wrote a letter ordering the killing of certain Companions. The author presents several independent arguments (wujooh): 

1. First, Uthman admitted ownership of the slave and camel but swore he did not write or order the letter. Every Muslim must accept his oath. 
2. Second, demanding the handover of Marwan (the alleged writer) is a sinful demand because Marwan denied writing it, and handing him over would lead to vigilante justice. 
3. Third, even if Uthman had admitted writing the letter, his blood would not be lawful (rebellion against a rightful imam is not justified by such an act). 
4. Fourth, even if his blood were lawful, the rebels had no authority to carry out punishment; only the imam can enforce penalties. 
5. Fifth, even if the letter were genuine, Uthman and Marwan would be blameless because the recipients deserved it due to their rebellion, siege, insults, and other crimes. The letter was a response to their aggression. 
6. Finally, the author laments that if the letter had reached its destination, the disastrous killing of Uthman and subsequent bloodshed might have been prevented, whereas allowing the rebels to proceed caused immense and lasting corruption.

الحدود
الغافقي
الكتاب

Misconception: If Uthman wrote the letter, his killing was justified.
Correction: Baqillani argues that even if the letter was genuine, the rebels' crimes justified it, and the killing was a greater corruption.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani say about the rebels' authority to punish Uthman?
A) They are allowed to execute punishment.
B) Only the imam can enforce punishments; the rebels are not trustworthy.
C) The rebels should have handed Uthman over to the authorities.
D) The rebels had no right to demand anything.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani argues that even if the letter was written, the rebels' crimes justified it, and their rebellion caused endless corruption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely excused your own anger or rebellion against those in authority by citing their faults. After reading Baqillani's argument, will you still claim your intentions justify your actions? What specific sin of yours, when exposed, will you now seek forgiveness for, rather than blame others?
?
Baqillani says the rebellion caused corruption that will not cease until the Day of Judgment. In what ways might your own role in conflict—whether in your family, workplace, or community—contribute to a legacy of division that outlasts you? What one step can you take today to repair a relationship you have damaged?
?
The rebels saw themselves as reformers. You may also believe you are on the side of truth while opposing others. What if you are wrong? Baqillani warns that even if the letter was genuine, the rebels' methods were disastrous. Is there a situation where you are so sure of your righteousness that you are willing to cause harm? How will you check yourself before you act?

The Legal Impossibility of Rebels Executing Punishment
Baqillani refutes the legal basis for killing Uthman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that Uthman's blood was not lawful and why the rebels could not execute punishment.

Core Idea
Even if Uthman admitted writing, his blood remained protected; rebels had no authority to punish.

Baqillani begins by addressing the accusation that Uthman wrote a letter. He argues that even if Uthman acknowledged the letter, his blood would not be lawful. Furthermore, even if his blood were lawful, the rebels—being a rabble—had no right to execute punishment. This lesson examines these legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Baqillani uses conditional statements.
2
Identify the two conditional scenarios
The passage presents two 'if' scenarios: if Uthman acknowledged the letter, and if his blood were lawful. Find both and notice the conclusion drawn from each.
3
Understand the role of the Imam
Baqillani mentions that the Imam cannot entrust punishment to the rebels. Reflect on why the Imam has this authority and why the rebels lack it.
This connects to the concept of legitimate authority in Islamic governance.
4
Ask: why does this matter?
Ask yourself why Baqillani emphasizes that the rebels are a 'rabble' (righa'). How does this affect the validity of their actions?
This helps understand the legal basis for not killing Uthman.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تسليمه إليهم ليتحكموا فيه فلو أنه أيضاً اعترف بالكتاب لم يحلّ دمه ولو حل أيضاً دمه لم تكن إقامة الحد لهم ولم يجز لإمام المسلمين أن يمكنهم من إقامة الحدود وهم رعاع ليس إليهم هذا الشأن ولا هم بمأمونين عليه. وعلى أنه لو ثبت أن عثمان ومروان كتبا الكتاب لم يكن ذلك بذنب لهما لأن أولئك القوم كانوا مستحقين له لسعيهم على عثمان وحصرهم له واستنفار الناس عليه وشتمه وتحصيبه على منبر رسول الله صلّى الله عليه وسلم ومنعه الماء واستخفافهم بسلطان الله وحصرهم الصحابة في منازلهم وتقدم الغافقي على سائرهم واستيلائهم على المدينة وبدون هذه الأفعال يُكتب بما كتب به عثمان فليت القوم انصرفوا وليت الكتاب وصل فإنه لم يكن والله أعلم يجري ما جرى من قتل عثمان وما أثمر من سفك الدماء بعده وما نحن في بقيته إلى اليوم فإن قتل أولئك كان من الصلاح في الدين وتمكينهم ما حاولوه من أعظم الفساد وقد أعقب من الشّتات والفرقة وسوء العاقبة ما لا ينقطع في غالب الظن إلى يوم القيامة
Handing him over to them so that they might control him. For if he also acknowledged the letter, his blood would not be lawful, and if his blood were lawful, the execution of the prescribed punishment would not be for them, and it is not permissible for the Imam of the Muslims to entrust them with the execution of punishments, for they are a rabble; this matter is not for them, nor can they be trusted with it. And even if it were established that Uthman and Marwan wrote the letter, that would not be a sin for them, because those people deserved it for their actions against Uthman: their siege of him, their inciting people against him, insulting him, throwing stones at him on the pulpit of the Messenger of God (peace be upon him), preventing him from water, belittling the authority of God, confining the Companions in their homes, and the precedence of al-Ghafiqi over the rest, and their takeover of Medina. Without these actions, they would not have been written about as Uthman wrote about them. Would that the people had dispersed, and would that the letter had arrived, for then — God knows best — what happened of the killing of Uthman and the resulting bloodshed after him, and what we are still suffering from its remnants to this day, would not have occurred. For killing those people would have been a reform in religion, while enabling them to achieve what they attempted was among the greatest corruption, and it has resulted in fragmentation, division, and ill consequences that, in all likelihood, will not cease until the Day of Resurrection.
Source pages p. 544

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the accusation that Uthman wrote a letter ordering the execution of some rebels. The author, Imam al-Baqillani, begins by rejecting the premise: even if Uthman had acknowledged the letter, his blood would not be lawful (i.e., the rebels would have no right to kill him). Moreover, even if his blood were lawful, the rebels themselves had no authority to carry out the punishment (hadd). The Imam of the Muslims would never entrust such authority to a rabble (righa'). The author then argues hypothetically: even if we accept that Uthman and Marwan wrote the letter, that would not be a sin because the rebels deserved harsh treatment due to their own crimes: they sieged Uthman, insulted him, threw stones at him on the Prophet's pulpit, prevented him from water, belittled God's authority, confined the Companions, and let al-Ghafiqi take precedence. Without such provocations, they would not have been written about. The author then laments that the rebels did not disperse and the letter did not arrive, because then the subsequent bloodshed would have been avoided. In fact, killing those rebels would have been beneficial for religion, while allowing them to pursue their rebellion resulted in fragmentation and evil that will persist until the Day of Judgment. This is a defense of Uthman's actions and a condemnation of the rebels.

الرعاع
الحد
الإمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Uthman's alleged letter justified his murder.
Correction: Baqillani shows that even if the letter existed, the rebels had no authority to punish him.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why can't the rebels execute the prescribed punishment (hadd) even if Uthman's blood were lawful?
A) Because Uthman was a caliph and immune
B) Because the rebels are a rabble and cannot be trusted
C) Because the punishment was too severe
D) Because Marwan wrote the letter

Key Takeaway
Even if Uthman had written the letter, the rebels had no right to punish him; the Imam cannot delegate punishment to a rabble.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been quick to condemn someone based on unverified accusations, like the rebels did with Uthman? How does this lesson's insistence on legal process and authority challenge your tendency to assume guilt?
?
If you were part of a group that believed someone had committed a serious wrong, but you did not have legitimate authority to act, would you feel justified in taking matters into your own hands? What does Baqillani's description of the rebels as 'rabble' (الرعاع) reveal about the danger of mob justice in your own life?
?
Think of a time when you acted without proper authority or knowledge, perhaps in judging or 'punishing' someone. How does the concept that even a ruler's admission does not permit vigilante action force you to re-examine your own sense of righteousness?

Uthman's Justification and the Consequences of Rebellion
Baqillani justifies Uthman's alleged letter and condemns the rebels' rebellion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that even if the letter existed, the rebels deserved it due to their crimes, and that killing them would have been reform.

Core Idea
The rebels' own crimes justified Uthman's actions; allowing them to rebel led to lasting corruption.

Baqillani continues his defense of Uthman by arguing hypothetically: even if the letter existed, the rebels deserved it because of their own transgressions. He then laments that the rebels did not disperse, because killing them would have been reform for religion, while their success led to ongoing division. This lesson explores these historical justifications and their consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the list of crimes Baqillani mentions.
This is a hypothetical argument: 'even if' the letter existed.
2
List the rebels' crimes
Baqillani enumerates several actions of the rebels that made them deserving of harsh treatment. Find and list them.
These include siege, insults, throwing stones, etc.
3
Understand the two hypothetical outcomes
Baqillani presents two 'what if' scenarios: if the rebels had dispersed, and if the letter had arrived. Then he states what would have happened if the rebels had been killed instead.
Killing them would have been reform; allowing them led to corruption.
4
Reflect on the lasting consequences
Baqillani says the consequences of not killing the rebels will last until the Day of Resurrection. Think about why he emphasizes this.
This shows the gravity of rebellion and the importance of decisive action.

Defending Uthman: Ijtihad and the Pulpit
Baqillani argues that Uthman's ascending the pulpit was not a sin and was done for valid ijtihad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that a leader's action, even if differing from the Prophet's practice, can be justified by ijtihad for public benefit and proper intention.

Core Idea
Valid ijtihad for public good can justify actions that seem to deviate from the Prophet's practice.

This lesson examines Baqillani's defense of Uthman against the accusation of disrespecting the Prophet's pulpit. Baqillani first questions the factual basis of the claim, then shifts to a principle: a leader's ijtihad for perceived benefit—like intimidating enemies or deterring those who covet the Imam's humiliation—can render an action permissible. He stresses that Uthman would never elevate himself over the Prophet, as that would be disbelief, so any change was done with veneration in mind.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if it were established... if his ijtihad led him...'.
Baqillani uses a two-part argument: factual denial followed by hypothetical justification.
2
Understand the key points of the argument
Identify the three benefits Baqillani lists for ascending the pulpit: intimidation of enemy, effective speech, and repressing those who covet humiliation of the Imam. Also note the emphasis on 'it would not make his blood lawful'—a serious claim.
Baqillani is countering a charge that could justify Uthman's killing.
3
Reflect on the principle of ijtihad and intention
Consider how Baqillani uses the concept of ijtihad and the Imam's discretion. He argues that as long as the action is not kufr (elevating oneself over the Prophet) and is done for a perceived benefit, it is not sinful.
This principle protects leaders from accusations based on actions that may have valid reasons unknown to others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تعلقهم بأنه رَقِيَ على المنبر فوق المرقاة التي كان يقوم عليها رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فإنه غير ثابت ولو ثبت لم يحل بذلك دمه ولم يكن ذلك من معاصيه إذا أداه اجتهاده إلى أن الصعود عليها مصلحة وأنه أرهب للعدو وأبلغ للقول وأقمع للطامع في إذلال الإمام ولعله أن يكون قد جرى ما أوجب ذلك مما لم نقف على كنهه وفي الجملة أنا نعلم أن عثمان لم يقدم نفسه على أبي بكر وعمر فضلاً عن الترفع على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لأن ذلك كفر من فاعله وإذا كان ذلك كذلك لم يكن في هذا تعلق لأحد إذا فعله الإمام لوجه من المصلحة يراه من تعظيمه لشأن الرسول عليه السلام
As for their claim that he ascended the pulpit above the step on which the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) used to stand, it is not established, and even if it were established, it would not make his blood lawful nor would it be among his sins, if his ijtihad led him to believe that ascending it was beneficial, that it was more intimidating to the enemy, more effective in speech, and more repressive to the one coveting humiliation of the Imam. Perhaps something occurred that necessitated it, the reality of which we are not aware. In sum, we know that Uthman did not elevate himself above Abu Bakr and Umar, let alone above the Prophet (peace be upon him), because that would be disbelief from its doer. Since that is the case, there is no argument for anyone if the Imam does it for a perceived interest, intending the veneration of the status of the Messenger (peace be upon him).
Source pages p. 545

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the accusation that Uthman once ascended the Prophet's pulpit to a step higher than where the Prophet used to stand. Baqillani first denies the factual basis: it is not established (ghayr thabit). Then he argues even if it were true, it would not make Uthman's blood lawful (i.e., justify his killing) nor would it be a sin, provided his ijtihad led him to consider it a benefit: it intimidates enemies, makes speech more effective, and curbs those who covet humiliating the Imam. Perhaps there was a reason unknown to us. Crucially, Baqillani asserts that we know Uthman never elevated himself over Abu Bakr and Umar, let alone the Prophet—such elevation would be kufr. Therefore, anyone criticizing this act has no argument if the Imam did it for a perceived interest while intending to venerate the Messenger. The passage defends Uthman by shifting focus to the permissible discretion of the Imam in matters of public interest.

Key Terms
المنبر
المرقاة
الاجتهاد
المصلحة
الكفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what would be the ruling if Uthman ascended the pulpit above the Prophet's step?
A) It would be an unforgivable sin.
B) It would be allowed if done for a justified ijtihad.
C) It would make his blood lawful.
D) It would be an act of kufr.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani defends Uthman by asserting that ijtihad for perceived public benefit, combined with the intention to venerate the Prophet, can justify an action that might otherwise appear disrespectful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see a leader or someone in authority doing something that appears to contradict the Prophet's example, do you immediately judge them, or do you pause to consider that they might be acting on a valid ijtihad for a greater good you don't see? How can you train yourself to withhold judgment and trust that their intention may still be to honor the Prophet?
?
This lesson shows that Uthman's action could be justified by his ijtihad. Is there a situation in your own life where you have condemned someone's action based on outward appearance without knowing their intention or the reasoning behind it? What would change if you gave them the benefit of the doubt like Baqillani did for Uthman?
?
If you were in a position of authority and faced a decision that might be misunderstood as disrespectful to the Prophet, would you have the courage to follow your ijtihad for a perceived benefit, knowing that others might accuse you? What fears might hold you back, and how does this lesson strengthen your resolve to act with sincerity and trust in Allah?

Uthman's Retreat at Hunayn: Tactical Maneuver, Not Flight
Baqillani refutes the claim of Uthman's flight at Hunayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's defense of Uthman by distinguishing between a tactical retreat and a flight of a fugitive.

Core Idea
Not all withdrawals are cowardice; some are strategic repositioning.

Some critics accused Uthman of fleeing the Battle of Hunayn. Baqillani refutes this by clarifying that his retreat was a calculated move, not a panic flight. He further notes that the entire army withdrew, leaving only the Prophet's closest relatives with him.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to get the general meaning.
Notice the contrast between 'انصراف منهزم' and 'انصراف متحرّف'.
2
Identify the two types of retreat
Find where Baqillani contrasts a fugitive's flight with a tactical maneuver. Underline the key phrases.
3
Analyze the supporting evidence
Baqillani supports his claim by stating that the entire army retreated and only the Prophet's uncle and son stayed. Why is this evidence important?
It shows Uthman was not alone in retreating.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: How does this argument clear Uthman of cowardice? What is the key distinction Baqillani makes?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تعلقهم بأنه انصرف يوم حنين، فإنه ما ثبت أنه انصرف انصراف منهزم، وإنما انصرف انصراف متحرّف لقتال ومنتهز الفرصة. وقد ذكر أن العسكر بأسره انصرف عن النبي صلّى الله عليه وسلم ولم يثبت معه إلا عمه العباس وابنه عبيد الله بن العباس أو قثم حين صاح: «يا للمهاجرين ! يا المقطوع بصدقه.
As for their claim that he retreated on the day of Hunayn, it is not established that he retreated the retreat of a fugitive; rather, he retreated the retreat of one maneuvering for battle and seizing an opportunity. It has been mentioned that the entire army retreated from the Prophet (peace be upon him), and only his uncle al-Abbas and his son 'Ubaydallah ibn al-Abbas, or Qutham, remained with him when he shouted, 'O Muhajirun! O the one whose truthfulness is certain!'
Source pages p. 545

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the accusation that Uthman fled from the Battle of Hunayn. Baqillani first denies that the retreat was a flight of a fugitive (insen yarmen li-ma'azim). Instead, it was a tactical repositioning (taharruf) for combat and seizing an opportunity. He then notes that the entire army withdrew from the Prophet, not just Uthman, and only the Prophet's uncle al-Abbas and his son remained with him when he shouted to rally the Muhajirun. By showing the general retreat and Uthman's intention to re-engage, Baqillani clears Uthman of cowardice and aligns him with the legitimate tactical response.

Key Terms
حنين
انهزام
تحرّف
منتهز الفرصة
المهاجرون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any withdrawal from battle is an act of cowardice.
Correction: Baqillani distinguishes between a dishonorable flight (انصراف منهزم) and a tactical maneuver (انصراف متحرّف) for combat advantage.

Checkpoint
How does Baqillani describe Uthman's retreat at Hunayn?
A) As a flight of a fugitive.
B) As a tactical maneuver for battle.
C) As an act of disobedience.
D) As a sign of fear.

Key Takeaway
Uthman's retreat at Hunayn was a strategic repositioning, not an act of cowardice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been falsely accused of cowardice or weakness when you were actually making a strategic decision? How did you defend yourself, and what does this lesson teach you about the importance of intention and context?
?
When you see someone withdraw from a difficult situation, what is your immediate judgment? How can you train yourself to withhold judgment until you understand the full context, as Baqillani did for Uthman?
?
Reflect on a time when you stayed silent or stepped back from a conflict. Was it out of fear or wisdom? How can you apply Baqillani's distinction between flight and tactical maneuver to evaluate your own actions?

Defense of Uthman's Favoritism Towards Relatives
Baqillani defends Uthman's generosity to relatives as a virtue, not a sin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani defends Uthman's generosity to relatives as a virtue rather than a sin, and how he uses the concept of ijtihad to explain any perceived shortcomings.

Core Idea
Uthman's favoritism was a sign of virtue, not sin.

In this passage, Baqillani addresses a common accusation against Uthman: that he showed undue favoritism to his relatives. He counters by listing Uthman's virtues and argues that caring for relatives is a praiseworthy quality. He even suggests that any perceived shortcoming is only a matter of leaving the more perfect course, not committing sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow of Baqillani's argument.
Notice the rhetorical question: 'So how can one imagine...'
2
Identify Baqillani's defense strategy
Baqillani first lists Uthman's undeniable virtues, then addresses the nepotism accusation head-on. Identify how each part of the passage serves the defense.
Look for the pivot from virtues to the specific accusation.
3
Note key Arabic terms
Write down and understand the meanings of الصهارة, الأهواء, البدع, الكلف, المحامل, الأولى, الشيخان. These are central to the argument.
الصهارة refers to kinship through marriage.
4
Understand 'ترك الأولى'
Baqillani concedes that Uthman might have left the more proper course, but argues this is not a sin. Understand why leaving the better option is not blameworthy.
This concept is crucial for evaluating actions of companions.
5
Reflect on the role of ijtihad
Consider how Baqillani uses the principle that each era has its own judgment to explain Uthman's choices. This shows respect for the companion's judgment.
Ijtihad is a valid basis for differing from the predecessors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا ما علم من سابقته وكثرة إنفاقه في سبيل الله وشرف منزله بالصهارة الثابتة له في ابنتي رسول الله وعظم مكانته في الدين والصفات الجميلة، والمآثر الحميدة على ما تضمنه فصل مناقبه، فكيف يتوهم فيه شيء مما ادعاه أهل الأهواء والبدع وأما كلفه بأقاربه وصلته إياهم وحبه الخير لهم فتلك صفة جميلة لم يودعها الله عزّ وجل إلا في خيار خلقه، وقد كان على مثل ذلك في بني هاشم على ما سنبينه في مناقب بني هاشم وقريش إن شاء الله تعالى وذلك محمود فيما لم يؤد إلى معصية. ولم يتحقق في شيء مما أتاه عثمان معصية بل له من المحامل الجلية الطاهرة ما يمنع من اعتقاد الحرمة. بل غاية ما في الباب أنه ترك الأولى، وما هو الأفضل اللائق به مما كان عليه الشيخان ولعله اعتقد أنه ما لا يشبه الأفضل في زمانه وعصره فلكل عصر حكم
This is what is known of his precedence, his abundant spending in the path of God, the honor of his status by the definite kinship through marriage to the two daughters of the Messenger of God, the greatness of his standing in religion, and his beautiful qualities and praiseworthy virtues, as contained in the chapter of his merits. So how can one imagine anything of what the people of whims and innovations claim about him? And as for his devotion to his relatives, his maintaining ties with them, and his love of good for them, that is a beautiful quality that God Almighty has only placed in the best of His creation, and he was upon the same regarding Banu Hashim as we will explain in the merits of Banu Hashim and Quraysh, God willing. That is praiseworthy as long as it does not lead to disobedience. And no sin has been verified in anything that Uthman did; rather, he has clear and pure interpretations that prevent the belief in prohibition. Rather, the utmost in the matter is that he left what is more proper and what is better and befitting him from what the two shaykhs were upon, and perhaps he believed that what he did was not inferior to the better in his time and era, for each era has its judgment.
Source pages p. 545

Commentary
This passage is a defense of Uthman against the accusation of nepotism. Baqillani begins by listing Uthman's undeniable virtues: his precedence, his spending in God's path, his honor as the husband of two of the Prophet's daughters, and his high status in religion. He then asks rhetorically how anyone can imagine faults in him. Specifically addressing the claim that Uthman favored his relatives, Baqillani argues that love and care for relatives is a beautiful quality that God places only in the best of His creation. He points out that Uthman was no different from the Banu Hashim in this regard. Such conduct is praiseworthy as long as it does not lead to sin, and no sin has been verified in any of Uthman's actions. Baqillani stresses that Uthman's deeds have clear and pure interpretations that preclude them being considered forbidden. At most, one could say Uthman left the more perfect course (al-awla) compared to the way of Abu Bakr and Umar. But he may have ijtihad that the better course for his own time was different, since each era has its own considerations. This effectively neutralizes the nepotism charge by recasting it as a commendable practice and explaining any perceived shortcoming as a matter of ijtihad.

Key Terms
الصهارة
الأهواء
البدع
الكلف
المحامل
الأولى
الشيخان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think any favoritism to relatives is wrong.
Correction: Baqillani shows it is a virtue unless it leads to sin, and Uthman's actions were not sinful.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the worst that can be said about Uthman's favoritism towards relatives?
A) It was a major sin.
B) It was an act of nepotism forbidden in Islam.
C) He left what was more proper (al-awla) compared to the two shaykhs.
D) He disobeyed God by favoring them.

Key Takeaway
Uthman's generosity to relatives was a virtue, not a sin, and any deviation from the ideal was due to valid ijtihad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were in Uthman's position and your every action was scrutinized by critics, could you say with certainty that you have never left the 'more proper' course? What does your defensiveness when criticized reveal about your reliance on God's judgment versus human approval?
?
Baqillani gives Uthman the benefit of the doubt, assuming ijtihad where there is no sin. When you see a fellow Muslim doing something you consider wrong, do you assume the best interpretation or rush to judge? Can you recall a specific instance where you failed to grant that benefit—and will you seek their forgiveness?
?
The lesson says that love for relatives is a beautiful quality God places in the best of people. If your heart feels resentment or envy toward your own family, what does that say about your spiritual state? Will you pray today for God to purify your heart and grant you that same noble affection?

Uthman's Absence from Badr: A Prophetic Command
Baqillani refutes the Badr absence allegation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Uthman's absence from the Battle of Badr does not diminish his virtue, and how the Prophet's command and acknowledgment refute the allegation.

Core Idea
Uthman's absence from Badr was by prophetic command, not cowardice.

Baqillani addresses the first objection against Uthman: his non-participation in the Battle of Badr. The author argues that absence does not imply lack of virtue, especially when commanded by the Prophet himself. This lesson explains the reasoning and the prophetic endorsements that vindicate Uthman.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how Baqillani begins by dismissing the objection as 'great ignorance' before presenting his arguments.
2
Identify the key arguments
List the three main points Baqillani uses: (1) imamate of the less excellent is permissible, (2) Uthman was commanded by the Prophet to stay, (3) the Prophet gave him the reward of participants. Underline each in the translation.
3
Reflect on the counterfactual
Consider Baqillani's point that if Uthman had stayed without excuse, the Prophet would have blamed him. This reinforces that the Prophet's silence implies approval.
This argument shifts the burden back to the objectors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تعلقهم بأنه لم يحضر بدراً فإنه جهل عظيم؛ لأن أكثر ما في أن يكون غيره أفضل منه وقد بينا أن إمامة المفضول جائزة مع حصول الفاضل بسبب عارض يقعده على أن الفضيلة له في تأخره عن بدر ثابتة لأنه إنما تأخر بأمر رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم له بذلك وإلزامه له لتعليل ابنته ومشاهدة أمرها لأن بنت الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم قد كانت مرضت فأمره بالقيام عليها وكان صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول: إن قوماً بالمدينة تخلفوا وما تخلفوا عما نحن فيه، قال الراوي: فكنا نرى أنه يعني عثمان وقد جعل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم له فضل الحاضرين وضرب له سهمه من غنيمة بدر ولو علم أنه مؤثر للتخلف عن الغزو لغير عذر لكان حرياً بذمه والتنبيه على فعله ورأيه فيه وهذا عائد بالطعن على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم والتهمة سوء له دون غيره. وأما تعلقهم بتأخره عن بيعة الرضوان فإنه غفلة وجهل ولجاج وعناد وذلك أنه إنما تأخر لتحمل رسالة النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إلى أهل مكة حتى قتل علي [؟] وقال الزبير استعمله علي على المدينة ثم إن قثم سار أيام معاوية إلى سمرقند مع سعيد بن عثمان بن عفان فمات بها شهيداً وكان يشبه النبي وروي أن ابن عباس نعى إليه أخوه قثم وهو في منزله فاسترجع وأناخ عن الطريق فصلى ركعتين فأطال فيهما الجلوس ثم قام إلى راحلته وهو يقرأ واستعينوا بالصبر والصلاة وإنها لكبيرة إلا على الخاشعين ولم يعقب قثم. حين أرجف بالعسكر أن قريشاً قتلت عثمان فغضب النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وقلق وقال: والله لئن كان قتلوه لأضرمنها عليهم ناراً؛ ثم أخذ لأجل هذا الخبر على الصحابة بيعة الرضوان وقال: هذه شمالي عن يمين عثمان وهي خير له من يمينه؛ فهو كان سبب بيعة الرضوان وغضب النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فكيف يكون بتأخره عنها منقوصا؟ فدل جميع ما وصفناه على فضل عثمان ونزاهته وعدالته وسلامة بيعته وأنه قتل مظلوماً.
As for their attachment (objection) that he did not attend Badr, it is great ignorance; because at most it would mean that someone else is better than him, and we have shown that the imamate of the less excellent is permissible when the more excellent is present due to an incidental hindrance, and moreover the virtue for him in his delay from Badr is established because he only delayed by the command of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) for that and his imposition upon him to attend to his daughter and oversee her affair, because the daughter of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) had fallen ill, so he ordered him to care for her, and the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to say: 'Indeed there are people in Medina who fell behind and they did not fall behind from what we are in.' The narrator said: 'We used to think that he meant Uthman, and the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) had given him the merit of those present and allotted him his share of the booty of Badr, and if he had known that he preferred to stay behind from the expedition without excuse, he would have been deserving of blame and censure for his action and opinion in it, and this would have been an attack on the Prophet (peace be upon him) and an evil accusation against him and not others.' As for their attachment (objection) regarding his delay from the Pledge of Ridwan, it is carelessness, ignorance, obstinacy, and stubbornness, because he only delayed to carry the message of the Prophet (peace be upon him) to the people of Mecca until Ali [?] was killed, and al-Zubayr said: 'Ali appointed him over Medina.' Then Qutham went during the days of Muawiya to Samarkand with Said ibn Uthman ibn Affan and died there a martyr, and he resembled the Prophet. It was narrated that Ibn Abbas was informed of the death of his brother Qutham while in his home, so he said 'Inna lillahi wa inna ilayhi rajiun' and halted by the roadside, prayed two rak'ahs in which he prolonged the sitting, then stood up to his mount reciting: 'And seek help through patience and prayer, and indeed it is difficult except for the humble' (Quran 2:45). Qutham left no descendants. When the army was alarmed by the rumor that Quraysh had killed Uthman, the Prophet (peace be upon him) became angry and anxious and said: 'By Allah, if they have killed him, I will set it ablaze upon them with fire.' Then he took the Pledge of Ridwan from the Companions on account of this news and said: 'This is my left hand of Uthman, and it is better for him than his right hand.' So he was the cause of the Pledge of Ridwan and the anger of the Prophet (peace be upon him), so how can he be diminished by his delay from it? All that we have described demonstrates the virtue of Uthman, his innocence, his justice, the soundness of his allegiance, and that he was killed wrongfully.
Source pages p. 546

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two objections raised against Uthman by his detractors. The author, Baqillani, addresses each objection in turn, presenting arguments to vindicate Uthman's character and actions. The first objection is that Uthman did not participate in the Battle of Badr. Baqillani responds that at most this would imply that someone else is more excellent, but the imamate of the less excellent is permissible when the more excellent is hindered by a valid excuse. Moreover, Uthman's absence was specifically by the command of the Prophet himself, who ordered him to stay behind to care for his daughter Ruqayya, who was ill. The Prophet acknowledged his merit by giving him the share of booty of a participant. If Uthman had stayed behind without excuse, the Prophet would have blamed him, but he did not. The second objection concerns Uthman's absence from the Pledge of Ridwan (Bay'at al-Ridwan) at Hudaybiyya. Baqillani responds that Uthman had been sent by the Prophet as an envoy to the Quraysh in Mecca. The rumor of his murder sparked the Prophet's anger and led to the pledge. Thus, Uthman was the direct cause of this momentous event, and the Prophet symbolically included him by placing his left hand on his right. Baqillani concludes that all these facts prove Uthman's virtue, innocence, justice, and that he was killed unjustly. The passage weaves together historical reports and theological reasoning to defend Uthman against the allegations of his opponents.

Key Terms
إمامة المفضول
الفضيلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Absence from Badr proves Uthman was less virtuous or cowardly.
Correction: His absence was by prophetic command to care for his daughter, and the Prophet rewarded him as if he were present.

Checkpoint
What was the main reason Baqillani gives for Uthman's absence from Badr?
A) Uthman was afraid of battle.
B) Uthman was ordered by the Prophet to care for his sick daughter.
C) Uthman was on a diplomatic mission.
D) Uthman was ill himself.

Key Takeaway
Uthman's absence from Badr was a virtuous act of obedience, not a failing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When someone accuses you of a fault, do you immediately seek to defend yourself, or do you first consider if there is a prophetic command or valid reason? This lesson shows that Uthman’s absence was by prophetic command. In your life, are there decisions others criticize that you made in obedience to Allah? How can you learn to trust that obedience to Allah’s commands is sufficient, even if others misunderstand?
?
The Prophet gave Uthman the share of booty even though he was not physically present. This shows that intention and obedience matter more than presence. Reflect on a time when you felt overlooked because you were not physically present for a good deed. How does this lesson change your perspective on the value of sincere intention?
?
Baqillani warns that accusing Uthman would actually be an attack on the Prophet. Are there instances where you have criticized a fellow believer without knowing the full context? How can you guard your tongue and assume the best of others, especially those known for virtue?

Uthman and the Pledge of Ridwan: The Cause of the Pledge
Baqillani refutes the Bay'at al-Ridwan absence allegation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Uthman's absence from Bay'at al-Ridwan actually enhances his virtue, as he was the cause of the pledge and honored by the Prophet.

Core Idea
Uthman's delay from the pledge was a prophetic mission that sparked the pledge itself.

Baqillani turns to the second objection: Uthman's absence from the Pledge of Ridwan at Hudaybiyya. He argues that Uthman was sent as an envoy to Quraysh, and the false rumor of his death angered the Prophet, leading to the pledge. This lesson shows how Uthman's role elevated rather than diminished his status.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the strong language ('carelessness, ignorance, obstinacy') used to introduce the refutation.
2
Understand Uthman's role as envoy
Uthman was sent to Mecca to deliver the Prophet's message. His absence was not by choice but by prophetic command.
The phrase 'until Ali [?]' is likely a corruption; focus on the clear meaning.
3
Connect the rumor to the pledge
The rumor that Uthman was killed prompted the Prophet's anger and the gathering for the pledge. Thus Uthman was the direct cause of this historic moment.
4
Note the Prophet's symbolic gesture
The Prophet placed his left hand on his right, saying it represents Uthman, showing his inclusion and honor.
