And Ali, peace be upon him, was driven to a great matter concerning the murderers of Uthman and those demanding his blood and those denying his killing. He could not establish retribution (qisas) and prescribed punishment on a people who killed a man while he did not know them by their identities, even though he knew they were under his protection and mixed with the innocent among the people of his army, in such a way that he could not distinguish them or attain knowledge of their identities. Nor could he exact retribution for the guardian, while they were a people at war with him, and they were not demanding blood for a specific person, nor did they have a clear proof, nor was there an admission or confession from anyone of killing like that which could be acted upon. And the protective supporters among his companions, such as [؟] Malik [؟] in a way that is sound, al-Ashtar al-Nakha'i, and Ibn Budayl ibn Warqa', and Ibn Saba', and Muhammad ibn Abi Bakr, and al-Ghafiqi, and others who held their view, they often criticized Uthman and spoke about him and disavowed him and those who supported him. And Ali, peace be upon him, could not take revenge on them and declare cursing...
Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of Ali's predicament regarding the killers of Uthman. It is incomplete but presents the core difficulty: The killers were mixed among the populace and not identifiable individually. Ali knew they were present but could not distinguish them. Legal requirements for retribution (a clear accuser, evidence, admission) were missing. Moreover, a faction among his own supporters (like al-Ashtar, Ibn Saba', etc.) were actively hostile to Uthman's memory and opposed any punishment. Thus, Ali was unable to exact justice without risking greater discord. The last sentence mentions Ali's inability to take revenge or declare cursing, but cuts off.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Ali faced pressure from both sides—yet he could not act. Have you ever been in a position where you knew a wrong needed correction but were paralyzed by conflicting loyalties or lack of clear means? What did you learn about yourself from that experience?

Refusers of Battle Did Not Deny Ali's Imamate
Baqillani argues that refusers of battle did not impugn Ali's imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Companions who declined to fight in the fitna did not necessarily reject Ali's legitimate imamate.

Core Idea
Refusal to fight is not automatically denial of leadership.

The issue of those who did not support Ali during the fitna is often misunderstood. Imam Baqillani clarifies that their reluctance arose from piety and fear of sin, not from impugning Ali's authority. This passage provides key examples from the Companions themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the repeated phrase 'he did not say: You are not an imam'.
This phrase is the key to Baqillani's argument.
2
Identify the three examples
Find the three Companions cited: an anonymous Companion, Muhammad (ibn Maslama), and Usama ibn Zaid. Note their specific statements.
Each one refrained from fighting but did not deny Ali's imamate.
3
Understand the reasoning
Baqillani shows that their refusals were based on fear of sin or prophetic commands, not on rejection of Ali's authority. This is his main point.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that such actions are not disparagement of the imamate. Think about how this defends the Companions' integrity.
This addresses a common sectarian accusation.

...and avoidance of sin in it and their assumption of obedience to sin in obedience to him in this action, so they argued against him regarding staying back, and they narrated to him reports about it. And one of them said: 'I will not fight until you bring me a sword with a tongue that knows the believer from the disbeliever and says: This is a believer, this is a disbeliever, so kill him!' He did not say: 'You are not an imam whose obedience is obligatory.' And Muhammad said to him after his consultation and objection: 'The Messenger of God (PBUH) entrusted to me that if a fitna occurs among the Muslims, I should break my sword and take instead a sword of wood.' And in another report: 'That I sheathe my sword and hold back in my house until a swift death or a mistaken hand comes to me. So beware, O Ali, lest you be that mistaken hand!' He did not say to him: 'You are not an imam whose obedience is obligatory.' Similarly, Usama ibn Zaid said to him: 'You know, O Ali, that if you entered the belly of a lion, I would enter with you, but there is no solidarity in the Fire.' He did not say: 'You are not an imam.' He only feared killing Muslims. And this is not a disparagement of the imamate at all.
Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This passage is a defense of those Companions who refrained from fighting alongside Ali (RA) during the fitna, particularly regarding the issue of Uthman's murder. The author, Imam Baqillani, argues that their refusal did not constitute a denial of Ali's imamate or an attack on his legitimate authority. He provides three examples: first, an anonymous Companion demanded a miraculous sword to distinguish believers from disbelievers before fighting, but did not question Ali's status; second, Muhammad (likely Muhammad ibn Maslama) cited a prophetic command to break his sword and stay home during fitna, again not denying Ali's imamate; third, Usama ibn Zaid declined to fight out of fear of killing Muslims, not because he rejected Ali's leadership. The author concludes that such actions are not disparagement of the imamate, but rather excusable precautions based on piety.

Why Ali Could Not Punish Uthman's Murderers
Baqillani explains why Ali could not execute legal punishment for Uthman's killers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the legal and political constraints that prevented Ali (RA) from carrying out qisas against Uthman's killers, as explained by Imam Baqillani.

Core Idea
Ali's inability to punish Uthman's killers was due to legal and practical impossibility, not approval.

Imam Baqillani addresses a key historical question: why did Ali (RA) not execute the prescribed legal punishment for Uthman's murder? This passage clarifies that Ali faced insurmountable obstacles — he could not identify the killers, lacked legal evidence, and feared political collapse. Baqillani's explanation refutes any suggestion that Ali condoned the killing.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to grasp Baqillani's argument.
2
Identify the obstacles
List the specific reasons Baqillani gives: unknown identities, lack of evidence, and political danger.
3
Consider the broader context
Reflect on the complex situation of the early Muslim community after Uthman's death.
4
Engage with the checkpoint
Answer the quiz question to check your understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد كان عليّ عليه السلام مدفوعاً إلى أمر عظيم من قتلة عثمان والمطالبين بدمه والمنكرين لقتله فكان لا يمكنه إقامة القود والحد على قوم قتلوا رجلاً لا يعرفهم بأعيانهم وإن كان يعلم أنهم تحت كنفه ومختلطون بالبراء من أهل عسكره من حيث لا يمكنه تمييزهم والوصول إلى معرفة أعيانهم ولا أن يُقيد للولي وهم أهل حرب له وغير مطالبين بالدم لأحد بعينه ولا مقيمين بيّنة على ذلك ولا حصل لهم إقرار ولا اعتراف من أحد بالقتل مثله ويمكن العمل به وكانت الحامية من أصحابه مثل ملك على وجه يصح الأشتر النخعي وابن بديل بن ورقاء وابن سبأ ومحمد بن أبي بكر والغافقي وغيرهم ممن يرى رأيهم يكثرون الطعن على عثمان والمقالة فيه والبراءة منه وممن تولاه . وكان عليّ عليه السلام لا يمكنه أن ينتقم منهم ويجرد القول في لعن قاتل عثمان والبراءة منه والأمر بإقصائهم وإخراجهم من العسكر لتمالئهم وتضافرهم وخوفه من إفساد الأمر عليه والقدح فيه ولا يلتحم ولا يلتئم ولا يمكنه التصويب لقتل عثمان وإظهار الرضا به لعلمه بأنه قتل مظلوماً وتبرئه من قتله وأنه لو قال ذلك اشتد نفور الناس عنه وكوشف واعتقد بذلك الضلال وظن السامعون أنه رأيه في عثمان وهو بريء من ذلك.
And Ali (peace be upon him) was driven to a great matter regarding the murderers of Uthman, those demanding his blood, and those denying his killing. He could not establish legal retaliation and punishment on a people who killed a man while not knowing them by their identities, even if he knew they were under his protection and mixed with the innocent among his army, in a way that he could not distinguish them or reach knowledge of their identities, nor could he exact retaliation for the guardian, and they were people at war with him, not demanding blood for anyone specific, nor providing evidence for that, nor did there occur for them a confession or acknowledgment from anyone of the killing like that which could be acted upon. And the partisans among his companions, such as al-Ashtar al-Nakha'i, Ibn Budayl ibn Warqa', Ibn Saba', Muhammad ibn Abi Bakr, al-Ghafiqi, and others who held their opinion, multiplied their criticism of Uthman, their discussion of him, their disavowal of him, and of those who supported him. And Ali (peace be upon him) could not take revenge from them nor openly pronounce curses on the killer of Uthman, disavow him, command their expulsion and removal from the army, due to their collusion and cooperation, and his fear of corrupting his affairs and disparagement of him, and it would not coalesce nor come together, nor could he approve of the killing of Uthman and show satisfaction with it, because he knew he was killed unjustly, and he disavowed his killing, and if he said that, people would strongly turn away from him and would uncover him and consider that a deviation, and listeners would think it was his opinion of Uthman, while he was innocent of that.
Source pages p. 558

Commentary
This passage is a historical and legal explanation of the difficult position Ali (RA) faced after Uthman's murder. The author, Baqillani, outlines the obstacles that prevented Ali from applying the prescribed legal penalties (qisas and hadd) against the murderers. First, Ali did not know the individual identities of the killers, even though he knew they were mixed among his troops. Second, the legal guardians (awliya) of Uthman's blood were his enemies, and they did not produce evidence or a confession. Third, among Ali's own supporters were many who openly criticized Uthman and disavowed him, making it politically dangerous to punish them. Ali feared that taking strong action would worsen dissent and harm his leadership. He also could not feign approval of the killing, knowing Uthman was innocent. Thus, his inaction was not due to weakness but to practical and legal constraints.

الحد
القصاص
الإمامة
الفتنة

C) He was afraid of the murderers.
D) He believed Uthman deserved it.

Key Takeaway
Ali could not punish Uthman's murderers because of unknown identities, lack of evidence, and fear of greater civil strife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you know someone is guilty but cannot prove it, how does that affect your sense of justice? Ali faced this exact dilemma. Have you ever been unable to right a wrong because of missing evidence? How did you handle the tension between wanting justice and accepting limits?
?
Ali refrained from punishing the killers because acting would have caused greater chaos. In your life, have you ever chosen inaction on a matter of principle because you saw that acting would only make things worse? Were you at peace with that choice, or did it weigh on you?
?
The passage reveals that Ali neither approved of Uthman's killing nor could he openly condemn it without risking further division. Are there beliefs you hold that you cannot express publicly because of the consequences? How does that situation challenge your integrity and reliance on Allah?

Interpreting Ali's Ambiguous Sayings: Method of Ta'wil
Baqillani defends Ali from charges of complicity in Uthman's murder by interpreting his ambiguous statements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani interprets ambiguous statements attributed to Ali (RA) to defend his innocence regarding Uthman's murder and how this reflects the broader methodology of ta'wil.

Core Idea
Ambiguous words demand charitable interpretation, not rash accusations.

Imam Baqallani examines statements made by Ali (RA) about the killing of Uthman (RA) that appear contradictory or incriminating. He demonstrates that when properly understood through the principles of ta'wil, these statements actually affirm Ali's innocence and his commitment to justice. This lesson introduces the subtle reasoning needed to harmonize ambiguous texts with known character and principles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Baqillani uses a very analytical style here.
2
Identify the two ambiguous statements
The passage presents two statements by Ali (RA): 'God killed Uthman and I am with him' and 'that does not sadden me nor gladden me'. Find each one and notice how Baqillani first reports the misinterpretation and then gives the correct ta'wil.
3

Baqillani shows that misinterpretation arises from taking words out of context or ignoring the speaker's known truthfulness. The correct meaning is derived by considering the speaker's character, other statements, and the overall intent. Reflect on how this method applies to other ambiguous texts.

And when he was asked about him, he would utter ambiguous speech and delve into subtle interpretation and gentleness with both parties. When they heard from him ambiguous speech and saw his killers mixed with his army, they thought he approved of what happened and that he was able to establish the punishment and exact retribution for his guardians, and that he was biased towards them, even though he was innocent of that. So the apparent mixing of the people with his army and what was heard from the ambiguous implications of his statements became a basis for the ijtihad of the one who fought him, demanding the blood of Uthman, and the one who stayed away from him, due to their assumption of what was far from him. So it is not unlikely that the one who fought him and the one who stayed away from him were correct in what they did because of this ambiguity, so sin is removed from whoever intended this intent, and did not seek by his fighting or staying away the abandonment of the imamate, or fitna, or belittling of the case, or love of plunder and raiding and taking what is not his. And it has been narrated that Ali (peace be upon him) said in Basra: 'By God, I did not kill Uthman nor did I assist in his killing, but God killed Uthman and I am with him.' This is ambiguous. Some people thought he deceived them and that he informed about himself that he killed him by his saying 'and I am with him.' But that is not the case, because he meant by it that He (God) caused him to die and will cause me to die with him, because he swore, and he is truthful, that he did not kill him nor assist in his killing. And it was narrated that he was asked about the killing of Uthman another time and said: 'By God, that does not sadden me nor gladden me.' This also became a way for the two parties to interpret him untruthfully. He only meant by his saying: 'It does not sadden me that someone demands his blood and strives in that to seek it from its proper door and way; nor does it gladden me that someone killed him.' How could it gladden him when it saddens him, and he says: 'O God, curse the killers of Uthman on land and sea.' So the interpretation of that is...
Source pages p. 559

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of the ambiguous statements attributed to Ali (RA) regarding the murder of Uthman (RA). Baqillani explains that Ali used cautious, ambiguous language to avoid inflaming the situation further. This led some to misinterpret his words as approval of the killing or even confession. However, the author provides the correct interpretation. First, Ali's statement 'God killed Uthman and I am with him' means that God caused Uthman's death and will also cause Ali's death, not that Ali participated. Second, when Ali said 'that does not sadden me nor gladden me,' he meant he was not saddened by those who sought justice properly, nor gladdened by the act of murder itself, as evidenced by his curse on the killers. The passage concludes that those who fought Ali or stayed away based on these ambiguities may be excused if their intention was not to undermine the imamate or cause fitna.

Correction: Baqillani shows that Ali's words, when properly interpreted, affirm his innocence and his concern for justice, not approval of the murder.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's method shows that ambiguous texts must be interpreted consistently with the known character and other statements of the speaker, avoiding hasty accusations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Ali's careful, restrained speech avoided further bloodshed. In your own life, when you face a conflict, do you rush to clarify your position with harsh words, or do you, like Ali, sometimes choose ambiguity for the sake of peace—and how do you ensure your silence or ambiguity is not misinterpreted?

The Problem of Ambiguous Statements and Justification
Baqillani analyzes how Ali's ambiguous words and the killers' presence created a legitimate doubt for fighters and refrainees.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how ambiguous speech combined with external circumstances can create a legitimate doubt (shubha) that may excuse those who act on that appearance.

Core Idea
Ambiguous words plus visible clues create a doubt that can excuse action if intentions are pure.

When Ali (RA) was asked about Uthman's murder, he used cautious, ambiguous language to avoid further fitna. However, the killers were seen among his army. Baqillani analyzes how this combination of ambiguous speech and visible presence created a genuine doubt that could excuse both those who fought Ali and those who refrained, as long as they did not intend evil. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice how Baqillani structures his argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key components
Look for: Ali's dilemma, his use of ambiguous speech, the killers' presence, and the people's perception. How do these interact?
3
Analyze the conclusion
Notice the condition: if the actor does not intend betrayal or sedition, the doubt removes sin. Ask yourself why this is significant for Islamic theology.
This relates to the concept of 'udhr bil-shubha.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاتل عثمان والبراءة منه والأمر بإقصائهم وإخراجهم من العسكر لتمالئهم وتضافرهم وخوفه من إفساد الأمر عليه والقدح فيه ولا يلتحم ولا يلتئم ولا يمكنه التصويب لقتل عثمان وإظهار الرضا به لعلمه بأنه قتل مظلوماً وتبرئه من قتله وأنه لو قال ذلك اشتد نفور الناس عنه وكوشف واعتقد بذلك الضلال وظن السامعون أنه رأيه في عثمان وهو بريء من ذلك. وكان إذا سئل عنه أورد الكلام المحتمل وتغلغل إلى لطيف التأويل والرفق بالفريقين وكانوا إذا سمعوا منه الكلام المحتمل ورأوا قتلته مختلطين بعسكره ظنوا أنه مؤثر لما جرى وأنـه متمكن من إقامة الحد وأخذ القصاص لأوليائه وأنه مُتَحَيِّفٌ لهم، وإن كان بريئاً ذلك؛ فيصير ظاهر اختلاط القوم بعسكره وما يُسْمَعُ من محتملات أقاويله من طريقاً لاجتهاد المحارب المطالب له بدم عثمان والقاعد عنه لموضع ظنهم به ما هو بعيد عنه فلا يبعد أن يكون المحارب لـه والقاعد عنه مصيباً فيما فعله لأجل هذه الشبهة فيزول الإثم عمن قصد هذا القصد، ولم يطلب بقتاله وقعوده عنه الخذلان للإمامة والفتنة والتهاون بالقصة وحب النهب والغارة وأخذ ما ليس له .
The killer of Uthman and disavowal from him, and the order to remove them and expel them from the army due to their collusion and cooperation and his fear of them corrupting his command and criticizing it, and it is not joined nor established, and he cannot approve the killing of Uthman and show satisfaction with it because he knows that he was killed unjustly and disavows from his killing, and that if he said that, people would strongly turn away from him and he would be exposed and believed thereby to be misguided, and the listeners would think it his opinion about Uthman while he is innocent of that. And when he was asked about him, he would utter ambiguous speech and delve into subtle interpretation and gentleness with the two groups. And when they heard from him ambiguous speech and saw his killers mixed in his army, they thought that he supported what happened and that he was able to establish the legal penalty and take retribution for his guardians, and that he was partial to them, even though he was innocent of that. So the apparent mixing of the people in his army and what was heard of the ambiguities of his statements becomes a way for the exertion of the one fighting him, demanding Uthman's blood, and the one refraining, due to their assumption about him what is far from him. So it is not unlikely that the one fighting him and the one refraining from him are correct in what they did because of this doubt, so the sin is removed from whoever intended this intention, and did not seek by his fighting or refraining to betray the imamate, cause sedition, treat the matter lightly, love plunder and raiding, and take what is not his.

Commentary
This passage is an analytical argument about how the ambiguous statements of Ali (رضي الله عنه) regarding Uthman's murder, combined with the visible presence of the killers in his army, created a legitimate doubt (شبهة) for both those who fought him and those who refrained from fighting him. The author explains that Ali avoided explicit condemnation or approval because it would cause further division. However, when people heard his ambiguous words and saw the killers among his ranks, they concluded that Ali was lenient or supportive of the killing, even though he was innocent. This external appearance (ظاهر) gave a basis for both the fighter and the refraining party to act based on their understanding. As long as they did not intend to harm the imamate or cause fitna, they are excused due to this doubt, and the sin is removed from them. The argument follows the sequence: Ali's dilemma, his cautious speech, the people's perception, and the consequence that their actions may be judged acceptable due to the prevailing doubt.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage justifies any action against Ali based on mere suspicion.
Correction: Baqillani specifies that it applies only when the person does not intend evil and acts on a plausible appearance, not on any arbitrary assumption.

Key Takeaway
Ambiguous speech, when combined with visible evidence, can create a legitimate doubt that excuses those who act on that appearance, provided their intention is not evil.

?
When you see someone acting on a mistaken assumption, do you rush to condemn them or consider that their intention might still be good? How can this lesson transform the way you react to misunderstandings in your community?

Ali's Statement: 'I Did Not Kill Uthman' Explained
Baqillani cites Ali's Basra speech and refutes its misinterpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes a misinterpretation of Ali’s statement about Uthman’s killing, clarifying the true meaning of 'with him'.

Core Idea
A famous saying of Ali was twisted by opponents; Baqillani restores its innocence.

Baqillani presents a specific narration that was used against Ali (رضي الله عنه). Some opponents claimed Ali confessed to killing Uthman. This lesson shows how Baqillani carefully explains the text to defend Ali's honour and truthfulness.

Guided Path
1
Read the narration in Arabic and translation
Examine Ali’s exact words and see how they were recorded. Notice the key phrase 'وأنا معه' (and I am with him).
The narration is from Basra, as cited by Baqillani.
2
Identify the ambiguous phrase and the misinterpretation
Realise that the phrase 'وأنا معه' was understood by some as a confession. Understand why they thought so.
The word محتمل (ambiguous) signals multiple possible meanings.
3
Understand Baqillani's refutation and correct explanation
See how Baqillani argues that 'معه' refers to dying together by God's decree, not participating in the murder. Note the emphasis on Ali’s truthfulness (صادق).
4
Reflect on the broader theological importance
Consider why defending Ali’s honour is crucial in Sunni theology and how careful textual interpretation protects against false accusations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد روي أن علياً عليه السلام قال بالبصرة: «والله ما قتلت عثمان ولا مالات على قتله ولكن الله قتل عثمان وأنا معه» وهذا محتمل؛ فظن قوم أنه خدعهم وأنه قد أخبر عن نفسه أنه قتله بقوله «وأنا معه»؛ وليس ذلك كذلك؛ لأنه أراد به أنه أماته ويميتني معه لأنه قد حلف وهو الصادق، أنه ما قتله ولا مالاً على قتله.
And it has been narrated that Ali (peace be upon him) said in Basra: 'By Allah, I did not kill Uthman nor did I assist in his killing, but Allah killed Uthman and I am with him.' And this is ambiguous. So some people thought that he deceived them and that he informed about himself that he killed him by his saying 'and I am with him'. But that is not the case, because he meant by it that He will cause him to die and will cause me to die with him, because he swore—and he is truthful—that he did not kill him nor assist in his killing.
Source pages p. 559

Commentary
This passage presents a specific narration from Ali (رضي الله عنه) and the false interpretation placed upon it. The text is a citation-and-explanation passage. The author first quotes the narration: 'I did not kill Uthman, I did not assist in his killing, but Allah killed Uthman and I am with him.' The word 'معه' (with him) was misinterpreted by some as a confession that Ali killed Uthman. Baqillani rejects this, explaining that the meaning is that God will cause both of them to die, and that Ali's oath affirms his innocence. The explanation follows the order: narration, misinterpretation, refutation, and correct interpretation based on Ali's truthfulness.

Correction: Baqillani explains that it means God will cause both of them to die, and Ali swore he did not kill him.

D) They thought Ali meant he will die soon.

Key Takeaway
Ali’s words were twisted by opponents, but Baqillani shows that they actually affirm his innocence and truthfulness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When others twist your words—as happened with Ali—do you seek to clarify the truth, or do you let the misunderstanding stand? How does Ali's truthful defense challenge you to defend the truth even when it costs you?
?
Ali accepted that God alone gives death and life. Are you living with that same conviction, or do you fear death as if it were in human hands? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed 'Allah killed Uthman and I am with him'?
?
The claim that Ali deceived his listeners was refuted by his character and oath. Is there any area of your life where your words might be misread, and are you living with the same consistency that Ali showed, so that your character would vindicate you?

Ali's Response: Neither Pleased Nor Displeased
Baqillani narrates another ambiguous response from Ali and defends its correct meaning against misinterpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct meaning of Ali's ambiguous statement about Uthman's killing and how Baqillani refutes false interpretations.

Core Idea
Ali's statement distinguishes the legitimate demand for justice from the murder itself.

Imagine being asked about a controversial event and your words are twisted. That's what happened to Ali regarding Uthman's killing. In this passage, Baqillani presents Ali's own words and shows how his real meaning was clear, yet opponents misinterpreted it. Let's read the passage and see how Baqillani defends Ali's position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the two parts of Ali's statement and the opposing interpretations.
2
Identify the false interpretations
Find where Baqillani says the two groups interpreted against Ali. What do they falsely claim?
One group might think Ali was indifferent, another that he was pleased.
3
Understand Baqillani's explanation
See how Baqillani breaks down Ali's words: 'what did not displease me' refers to the legitimate demand, and 'did not please me' refers to the murder.
He supports this with Ali's own curse on the killers.
4
Examine the counter-interpretation
Read the part where the opposing view claims Ali's curse was insincere. Why does Baqillani reject this?
Consider how this aligns with the core idea.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وروي أنه سئل عن قتل عثمان مرة أخرى فقال: «والله ما ساءني ذلك ولا سرني»؛ فصار هذا أيضاً طريقاً لتأويل الفريقين عليه غير الحق؛ وهو إنما أراد بقوله: «ما ساءني مطالبة من يطالب بدمه واجتهاده في ذلك لطلبه له من بابه ووجهه؛ ولا سرني قتل من قتله؛ وكيف يسره يسوؤه وهو يقول: «اللهم العن قتلة عثمان في البر والبحر»؛ فيقول التأويل عليه أراد بقوله: «اللهم العنهم إن كانوا مستحقين للعن وكان عثمان غير مستحق للقتل، ليرضى العامة بذلك؛ وإلا فهو الذي سبب قتله وألب عليه، وكان ذلك من غرضه لظلم عثمان وغضبه الأمر.
And it was narrated that he was asked about Uthman's killing another time and said: 'By Allah, that did not displease me nor did it please me.' So this also became a way for the two groups to interpret against him in a false manner. And he only meant by his saying: 'What did not displease me is the demand of the one demanding his blood and his exertion in that to seek it from its proper door and way; and what did not please me is the killing of the one who killed him. And how could it please him while it displease him and he says: O Allah, curse the killers of Uthman on land and sea?' Then the interpretation against him says: He meant by his saying 'O Allah, curse them' if they are deserving of curse and Uthman was undeserving of killing, to please the public with that; otherwise, he is the one who caused his killing and instigated against him, and that was from his purpose due to Uthman's tyranny and his anger regarding the matter.
Source pages p. 559

Commentary
This passage presents a second narrated response from Ali and the false interpretation attached to it. The author quotes Ali's statement that the killing neither displeased nor pleased him. The author then provides the correct interpretation: Ali meant that the legitimate demand for blood did not displease him, but the actual murder did not please him. The author supports this by citing Ali's own curse upon the killers of Uthman, which shows his displeasure. The passage then reports the opposing interpretation that Ali's curse was insincere, meant only to appease the public, and that he was actually responsible for the killing. The argument follows the order: narration, false interpretation, correct interpretation, evidence from another hadith, and the opposing view.

Key Terms
تأويل
محتمل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ali was indifferent to Uthman's murder or pleased with it.
Correction: Baqillani explains that Ali's words refer to the legitimate pursuit of blood-money, not the murder itself, and his curse on the killers shows his displeasure.

Checkpoint
What evidence does Baqillani use to show that Ali was displeased with Uthman's killing?
A) Ali's statement that he was sad.
B) Ali's curse upon the killers.
C) Ali's tears.
D) Ali's refusal to eat.

Key Takeaway
Ali's statement about Uthman's killing must be understood in context: his displeasure was at the murder itself, not at the demand for justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ali’s statement was twisted by people who wanted to fit him into their own narrative. When you hear a rumor or ambiguous statement about someone you already dislike, do you instinctively accept the worst interpretation? How can you train yourself to seek the charitable reading that Baqillani models here?
?
The passage shows that Ali’s actions (his curse) aligned with his words—he was consistent. But in your own life, are there areas where your public words and private actions contradict each other? What would it cost you to align them fully?
?
Baqillani defends Ali against false interpretations by returning to the primary sources. When you find yourself in a disagreement, do you rely on echo chambers or do you go back to the original evidence? Be honest—what is one relationship or opinion you would need to re-examine if you did that today?

Ali's Statement: Uthman's Blood in My Head
Baqillani cites Ali's saying about Uthman's blood and explains two conflicting interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two conflicting interpretations of Ali's statement regarding Uthman's blood and why the correct one is about responsibility for retribution.

Core Idea
Ali's words were about legal responsibility, not confession.

In this passage, Baqillani presents a famous statement attributed to Ali and the two ways it has been understood. The first interpretation, accepted by the people of truth, sees Ali as accepting the duty to pursue justice. The second, promoted by critics, falsely accuses him. Understanding this distinction clarifies Ali's stance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: a narration followed by two interpretations.
The phrase 'دم عثمان في جمجمتي' is key.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Locate where 'فقال أهل الحق' and 'وقال آخرون' appear. Identify what each group claimed Ali meant.
The first interpretation is about legal retribution; the second is an accusation of guilt.
3
Understand the correct view
Reflect on why the people of truth interpreted the statement as they did. Consider the concept of 'دم في عنق' as an obligation to seek justice.
This clarification defends Ali's innocence.

وسئل فيما حكي عثمان فقال: إن دم عثمان في جمجمتي هذه؛ فقال أهل الحق: إنه أراد أن دمه في عنقي، ولازم لي وواجب علي حتى أقيد به إذا قامت البينة عليهم بأعيانهم وأمكن أخذ الحق منهم. وقال آخرون: لا! بل أراد أنه هو الذي قتله وأمر بذلك ودعا إليه، وكذلك كان يجب عليه.
And he was asked, as narrated, about Uthman's blood, so he said: Verily Uthman's blood is in this head of mine. Then the people of truth said: He meant that his blood is on my neck, binding upon me and obligatory upon me, until I exact retribution for it when the proof is established against them specifically and the right can be taken from them. And others said: No! Rather he meant that he is the one who killed him, commanded that, and called to it, and that was required of him.
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage is a citation-and-explanation passage presenting a third narrated statement from Ali and two opposing interpretations. The author quotes Ali saying 'Uthman's blood is in my head.' The first interpretation, attributed to 'the people of truth,' is that this means Ali is responsible for carrying out the legal retribution (القصاص) once the killers are identified through clear proof. The second interpretation, offered by critics, is that Ali meant he himself killed Uthman or orchestrated the killing. The passage simply presents both views without further comment, following the sequence: narration, correct interpretation, and false interpretation.

Key Terms
دم عثمان
دم في عنق
بينة
أقيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ali's statement is a confession that he killed Uthman.
Correction: The correct interpretation, held by the people of truth, is that Ali meant he bears the responsibility to exact retribution, not that he committed the murder.

Checkpoint
According to the 'people of truth', what did Ali mean by 'Uthman's blood is in my head'?
A) He admits to killing Uthman.
B) He is obliged to seek retribution for Uthman's blood.
C) He feels guilty about Uthman's death.
D) He is thinking about Uthman.

Key Takeaway
Ali's statement about Uthman's blood is properly understood as an acceptance of the duty to see justice done, not a confession of guilt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ali's words could be twisted into a false accusation. When have you allowed a misunderstanding of someone's words to lead you to a wrong judgment about them? How will you now guard against that?
?
The correct interpretation of Ali's statement required looking at his character and deeds. Is there anyone in your life whose actions you have judged based on a single statement, without considering the full context? Will you seek to understand them better?
?
This lesson shows that even a great figure like Ali could have his words misinterpreted to cast doubt on his innocence. How does that challenge you to be more careful in how you interpret the statements of scholars or leaders today? What step will you take to verify before accepting an accusation?

Ali's Ethical Rules of Engagement
Baqillani relates Ali's commands to his soldiers to exercise restraint and follow ethical rules of engagement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific ethical commands Ali (رضي الله عنه) gave to his army before the Battle of the Camel, emphasizing restraint and justice.

Core Idea
Ali's army was commanded to show extreme restraint even in war.

This passage presents a historical narration of Ali (رضي الله عنه) giving instructions to his army before the Battle of the Camel at Basra. It highlights his emphasis on restraint, justice, and ethical conduct even toward opponents. The following Arabic text contains two key speeches that set the rules of engagement.

Guided Path
1
Read the full Arabic passage
Carefully read the Arabic text of Ali's sermon. Notice the two separate speeches and the specific commands he gives.
The passage includes two occasions where Ali addressed the army.
2
Identify the key commands
List the specific prohibitions and permissions Ali gives regarding treatment of the enemy, property, and women.
Pay attention to commands like 'do not pursue', 'do not mutilate', etc.
3
Reflect on the ethical principles
Consider why Ali gave these commands. What does this teach about Islamic ethics in warfare? Connect to the idea of restraint even when facing those who insult.
This shows that Islamic warfare is not about aggression but about justice and mercy.

وقد روي أن علياً، عليه السلام قام في الناس خطيباً عند مسيره إلى البصرة فقال: «أيها الناس! املكوا أنفسكم وكفوا أيديكم وألسنتكم عن هؤلاء؛ فإنهم إخوانكم؛ واصبروا على ما نابكم؛ فإن المخصوم من خصم اليوم وسار على تعبئته»؛ وأنه قام فيهم مقاماً آخر فقال: لا تقاتلوا القوم حتى يبدؤوكم فإنكم بحمد الله على حجة وإذا قاتلتموهم فغلبتموهم فلا تجهزوا على جريحهم وإذا هزمتموهم فلا تتبعوا مدبراً ولا تكشفوا عورة ولا تمثلوا بقتيل فإذا وصلتم إلى رحال القوم فلا تهتكوا ستراً، ولا تدخلوا داراً إلا بإذن ولا تأخذوا شيئاً من أموالهم إلا ما تجدونه في عسكرهم يعني من سلاح وخزائن السلطان، فإنه قد بين ذلك وفسره في كلام آخر قد ذكرناه في غير هذا الكتاب «ولا تهجوا امرأة وإن شتمن أعراضكم وسببين أمراءكم وصلحاءكم فإنهن ضعاف القوى والأنفس والعقول ولقد كنا نؤمر بالكف عنهن وإن كن مشركات».
And it has been narrated that Ali (peace be upon him) stood among the people as a preacher during his march to Basra and said: 'O people! Control yourselves and restrain your hands and tongues from these people, for they are your brothers. And be patient with what has befallen you, for the defeated is one who is defeated today and continues on his mobilization.' And that he stood among them in another station and said: 'Do not fight the people until they start with you, for you, by the praise of Allah, are upon a proof. And if you fight them and overcome them, do not finish off their wounded. And if you defeat them, do not pursue a fleeing person, do not expose a private part, do not mutilate a dead body. And when you reach the dwellings of the people, do not tear a curtain, do not enter a house except with permission, and do not take anything from their wealth except what you find in their camp, meaning weapons and the treasuries of the ruler. For he had clarified and explained that in another speech which we have mentioned elsewhere: 'And do not satirize a woman, even if they insult your honor and abuse your commanders and righteous ones, for they are weak in strength, souls, and intellects. And we were commanded to refrain from them even if they are polytheists.'
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage presents a historical narration of Ali (رضي الله عنه) giving instructions to his army before the Battle of the Camel at Basra. It is a hadith citation with added explanation. The passage consists of two quotes from Ali. First, he urges self-control and patience, calling the opponents 'your brothers.' Second, he gives detailed rules of engagement: do not initiate fighting, do not kill the wounded, do not pursue fleeing soldiers, do not mutilate bodies, respect private property, and do not insult women even if they insult you. The author adds a note that these rules were further explained elsewhere. The passage shows Ali's ethical conduct in war, emphasizing restraint and justice.

Key Terms
حجة
تعبئة
أمراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Ali's army was aggressive and attacked without restraint.
Correction: This sermon shows Ali explicitly forbade aggression and set strict ethical boundaries, even commanding patience and calling opponents brothers.

Checkpoint
According to Ali's sermon, what should be done if a soldier flees after being defeated?
A) Pursue and capture him.
B) Do not pursue him.
C) Kill him if possible.
D) Insult him as he runs.

Key Takeaway
Ali's commands demonstrate that Islamic warfare is governed by strict ethical rules, including restraint even toward enemies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue with someone you disagree with, do you control your tongue as Ali commanded? Think of a recent conflict—did you say something that dehumanized the other person? Will you ask their forgiveness and commit to speaking only what is just, even when provoked?
?
Ali forbade mutilating dead bodies, even in war. What does that say about the dignity of the human being? Is there someone you have ‘mutilated’ with your words after they have left your life—through gossip, slander, or bitterness? Will you stop and seek to repair that?
?
Ali commanded not to insult women even if they insult your honor. What is the state of your heart when you are insulted? Do you retaliate or trust that Allah will defend your honor? Today, when someone insults you, will you remain silent and let the proof be on your side, as Ali said?

Ali Did Not Declare His Opponents Disbelievers
Baqillani narrates Ali's words and actions to prove his opponents remained Muslims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqillani uses the words and actions of Ali ibn Abi Talib (رضی الله عنه) to establish that his opponents in the civil wars were not declared disbelievers, but were considered Muslims and brothers.

Core Idea
Ali considered his opponents Muslims, not apostates.

This passage presents a central argument from Sunni theology: that despite the bitter conflicts among the Companions, they remained Muslims. Imam al-Baqillani narrates three key incidents from Ali's life to prove that Ali himself did not label his adversaries as disbelievers. As we read the passage, notice how Ali's own commands and statements serve as evidence for this important principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage begins with a discussion about Uthman's blood, then moves to three specific incidents.
2
Identify the three incidents
Find and label the three key incidents: the sermon at the march to Basra, the dialogue with al-A'war ibn Aban, and the speech upon returning from Sham addressing the Khawarij.
Each incident contains explicit statements from Ali about the status of his opponents.
3
Analyse the evidence
For each incident, note what Ali said or commanded. How do these words show that he regarded his opponents as fellow Muslims?
Pay special attention to the phrase 'إخوانكم' (your brothers) and the instructions not to harm the wounded or pursue retreating fighters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عليه أراد بقوله : «اللهم العنهم إن كانوا مستحقين للعن وكان عثمان غير مستحق للقتل، ليرضى العامة بذلك؛ وإلا فهو الذي سبب قتله وألب عليه، [؟] وقد كان ذلك من غرضه لظلم عثمان وغضبه الأمر. وسُئل فيما حكي عثمان فقال : إن دم عثمان في جمجمتي هذه؛ فقال أهل الحق : إنه أراد أن دمه في عنقي، ولازم لي، وواجب علي حتى أقيـد بـه قامت البيئة عليهم بأعيانهم وأمكن أخذ الحق منهم. وقال آخرون : لا ! بل أراد أنه هو الذي قتله وأمر بذلك ودعا إليه؛ وكذلك كان يجب عليه. فكل هذه الأقاويل مع اختلاط القتلة بأهل عسكره وكونهم تحت رايته ليسوغ التأويل للمحارب له والقاعد عنه عند يقينه وتشككه وكان علي عليه السلام أبصر وأعلم بما يعرض لهم من الشبهات وكان لا يجبر القاعد عنه على الحرب علماً منه بما سبق إلى وهمه مما هو بريء منه. وقد روي أن علياً، عليه السلام قام في الناس خطيباً عند مسيره إلى البصرة فقال: «أيها الناس! املكوا أنفسكم وكفوا أيديكم وألسنتكم عن هؤلاء؛ فإنهم إخوانكم؛ واصبروا على ما نابكم؛ فإن المخصوم من خُصِمَ اليوم وسار على تعبئته»؛ وأنه قام فيهم مقاماً آخر فقال: لا تقاتلوا القوم حتى يبدؤوكم فإنكم بحمد الله على حجة وإذا قاتلتموهم فغلبتموهم فلا تجهزوا على جريحهم وإذا هزمتموهم فلا تتبعوا مُدبراً ولا تكشفوا عورة ولا تُمثلوا بقتيل فإذا وصلتم إلى رحال القوم فلا تهتكوا ستراً، ولا تدخلوا داراً إلا بإذن ولا تأخذوا شيئاً أموالهم إلا ما تجدونه في عسكرهم يعني من سلاح خزائن السلطان، فإنه قد بين ذلك وفسره في كلام آخر قد ذكرناه في غير هذا الكتاب «ولا تهجوا امرأة وإن شتمن أعراضكم وسببين أمراءكم وصلحاءكم فإنهن ضعاف القوى والأنفس والعقول ولقد كنا نُؤْمَرُ بالكف عنهن وإنهن مشركات». وهذا يدل على أنهن عنده غير مشركات وإن رأين حربه وأنهن مع هذه الحال مؤمنات. وكذلك قوله : «فإنهم إخوانكم يدل على أنهم مسلمون ليسوا بكفار ولا فساقٍ أيضاً. ولأن مثل هذا الإطلاق يقتضي المساواة عنده في الرتبة والمنزلة الدينية. وقد روى أن الأعور بن أبان المنقري وكان من أماثل أصحاب عليّ رضي الله عنه قام إليه في مسيره إلى البصرة فقال يا أمير المؤمنين علام تَقَدِّمُنا؟ فقال: على الإصلاح وإطفاء الثائرة، لعل الله شمل هذه الأمة يجمع ويضع حربهم، وقد أجابوا»، قال: «فإن لم يجيبوا؟» قال: «تركناهم ما تركونا»، قال فإن لم يتركونا؟» قال: «دفعناهم عن أنفسنا»، قال: فهل لهم مثل ما عليهم من هذا؟ قال: «نعم». وروي رواية ظاهرة أن عليا قام عند مُنصَرَفه من الشام وخروج الخوارج عليه وإنكارهم التحكيم وإكفارهم معاوية وأهل الشام والبصرة عند خطاب ورأي أشارت عليه به همذان وجارية بن قدامة التميمي، فقال عند ذلك : «إنا والله ما قاتلنا أهل الشام على ما توهم هؤلاء الضلال من التكفير والفراق في الدين وما قاتلناهم إلا لنردهم إلى الجماعة وأتاكم هذا منهم في الفُرْقة وإنهم لإخواننا في الدين قبلتنا واحدة ورأينا أننا على الحق ودونهم وإني لعلى عهد من رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وأمر أمرني فيه بقتال الباغين والناكثين وإن الرشاد عندي أن يجمعنا الله وإياهم وما لهذا الأمر مثل الرفق عسى الله أن هذه الفرقة إلى ما كانت عليه من الجماعة فما كره الصلح من هؤلاء يجمع الضلال إلا كان يكره الجهاد للعدو ويَضنُّون بأنفسهم عن الحرب ويريدون من الاعتداء على المسلمين لقد كنا مع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وإن القتل بين الآباء والأبناء والإخوان وذوي القرابات فما نزداد على كل مصيبة وكل شدة
He intended by his statement: 'O Allah, curse them if they are deserving of curse and if Uthman was not deserving of killing, to satisfy the common people thereby; otherwise, he is the one who caused his killing and incited against him.' And that was from his purpose due to Uthman's oppression and his anger about the matter. And he was asked regarding what was narrated about Uthman, and he said: 'The blood of Uthman is in this skull of mine.' So the people of truth said: He meant that his blood is upon my neck, binding upon me, and obligatory upon me until I exact retribution if evidence is established against them by name and it becomes possible to take the right from them. Others said: No! Rather he meant that he is the one who killed him, commanded that, and called for it; and thus it was obligatory upon him. All these sayings, despite the mixing of the killers among the people of his army and their being under his banner, do not justify interpretation for the one who fought him or the one who stayed away from him when he is certain or doubtful. And Ali, peace be upon him, was more discerning and more knowing of what occurs to them of doubts, and he would not compel the one who stayed away from him to go to war, knowing what had preceded into his imagination of what he is innocent of. And it has been narrated that Ali, peace be upon him, stood among the people as a speaker during his march to Basra and said: 'O people! Control yourselves and restrain your hands and tongues from these people; for they are your brothers; and be patient with what has befallen you; for the vanquished is the one who is defeated today and continues in his formation.' And he stood among them in another position and said: Do not fight the people until they start fighting you, for you are, by the praise of Allah, upon proof. And if you fight them and defeat them, do not finish off their wounded, and when you defeat them do not pursue a retreating one, nor expose a private part, nor mutilate a slain one. When you reach the dwellings of the people, do not tear a veil, nor enter a house except with permission, nor take anything from their wealth except what you find in their camp, meaning from the weapons of the treasuries of the ruler. For he explained and detailed that in another speech which we have mentioned in other than this book: 'And do not revile a woman even if they insult your honor and curse your leaders and righteous ones, for they are weak in strength, souls, and intellects, and indeed we were commanded to refrain from them even if they are polytheists.' And this indicates that in his view they are not polytheists even if they support his war, and that they are believers in this state. Likewise his statement: 'For they are your brothers' indicates that they are Muslims, not disbelievers nor even wicked. And because such an unrestricted expression necessitates equality in his view in religious rank and station. And it has been narrated that al-A'war ibn Aban al-Minqari, who was among the distinguished companions of Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, stood before him during his march to Basra and said: O Commander of the Faithful, for what purpose do you lead us? He said: For reconciliation and extinguishing the embers that it may be that Allah will unite this Ummah and put an end to their war, and they have responded. He said: And if they do not respond? He said: We will leave them as long as they leave us. He said: And if they do not leave us? He said: We will repel them from ourselves. He said: Then do they have the same as what is upon them from this? He said: Yes. And it has been narrated, a well-known narration, that Ali stood at his return from Sham and the emergence of the Khawarij against him and their denial of arbitration and their declaring disbelief of Mu'awiya and the people of Sham and Basra, upon a speech and opinion suggested to him by Hamdan and Jariya ibn Qudama al-Tamimi. He said upon that: 'By Allah, we did not fight the people of Sham upon what these misguided ones imagine of declaring disbelief and separation in religion. We only fought them to return them to the community and this came to us from them in separation. And they are our brothers in religion; our qibla is one; and we see that we are upon the truth and they are below it. And I am upon a covenant from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, and a command with which he commanded me about fighting the transgressors and the violators of oaths. And right guidance in my view is that Allah will unite me and them. There is nothing for this affair like gentleness. Perhaps Allah will return this group to what it was upon of community. Whoever dislikes reconciliation from these people gathers misguidance except that he dislikes jihad against the enemy and is miserly with himself regarding war and desires aggression against the Muslims. We were with the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, and killing occurred among fathers, sons, brothers, and relatives, and we only increased in every calamity and every hardship.'
Source pages p. 560

Commentary
This passage is a historical and theological argument presented by Imam al-Baqillani to demonstrate that Ali ibn Abi Talib (رضي الله عنه) considered his opponents in the civil wars (the people of Basra, Sham, and the Khawarij) to be Muslims, not disbelievers. The passage begins with a discussion of Ali's statement about Uthman's blood, where different interpretations are mentioned. It then narrates three key incidents: the sermon at the march to Basra, the dialogue with al-A'war ibn Aban, and the speech upon returning from Sham. In the first incident, Ali explicitly calls the opponents 'your brothers' and gives strict orders not to initiate fighting, not to kill the wounded, not to pursue retreating fighters, and not to harm women, even if they insult them. He then comments that these women, though supporting the war, are not considered disbelievers; rather they are believers. The author explains that this shows Ali regarded them as Muslims. In the second incident, when asked about the purpose of the march, Ali replies 'reconciliation and extinguishing the embers,' and when asked if the opponents have the same standing, he answers 'yes,' implying they are not outside the fold of Islam. In the third incident, addressing the Khawarij who declared Mu'awiya and his followers disbelievers, Ali explicitly denies that he fought them for that reason; he only fought to return them to the community (al-jama'a) and calls them 'our brothers in religion' with the same qibla. He reiterates his commitment to fighting 'transgressors and violators of oaths' based on the Prophet's command, but not to declare them disbelievers. Throughout, al-Baqillani uses these narrations to refute any notion that those who fought against Ali were considered apostates by him.

Key Terms
مخالفيه
إخوانكم
التكفير
الباغين
الناكثين
الجماعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because Ali fought against Aisha, Talha, and Zubayr, or against Muawiya, he considered them disbelievers.
Correction: The passage shows Ali explicitly called them 'brothers' and prohibited killing the wounded, pursuing retreat, etc., and stated his aim was reconciliation, not takfir.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was Ali's stated goal in marching to Basra?
A) To kill the leaders of the opposition
B) To force them to accept his caliphate
C) To achieve reconciliation and extinguish the embers of war
D) To avenge Uthman's death

Key Takeaway
Ali's own words and actions demonstrate that those who fought him remained Muslims, contrary to later Khawarij claims.

Ali's Stance on Takfir of Opponents
Baqillani cites two narrations from Ali to refute extremist takfir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses Ali's own words to argue against declaring Muslim opponents as disbelievers, and the correct Sunni stance on judging conflicts among Companions.

Core Idea
Ali fought to reunite, not to excommunicate.

This passage from Baqillani's Tamhid presents two key reports from Ali that directly contradict the extremist positions of the Khawarij and some Shi'a who declared Ali's opponents as kuffar. By examining Ali's own statements, Baqillani establishes the correct Sunni doctrine that internal conflicts among Muslims do not warrant takfir. The first narration shows Ali's intention for reconciliation, while the second explicitly denies fighting for takfir and affirms brotherhood.

Guided Path
1
Read the two narrations
Start by reading the full Arabic text and translation. The first narration is a dialogue with al-A'war; the second is a longer speech after the Khawarij emerged. Notice the context and key phrases.
The second narration is more detailed and includes explicit denial of takfir.
2
Identify Ali's stated motives
In the first narration, Ali says he seeks 'reconciliation and extinguishing the spark'. In the second, he says 'we only fought them to bring them back to the community'. Highlight these phrases.
3
See how Baqillani uses the reports
After the quote, Baqillani states that Ali's words are contrary to what the Shi'a and Khawarij believe. He concludes that there is no excuse for extremism in takfir. Understand how the evidence refutes the misconception.
Baqillani is using the authority of Ali to settle the issue.
4
Reflect on the lesson's application
Consider how this principle applies to later sectarian conflicts. The lesson teaches restraint in judging fellow Muslims, especially when interpretation (ta'wil) is involved.
This is a foundational Sunni principle.

وقد روى أن الأعور بن أبان المنقري وكان من أماثل أصحاب عليّ رضي الله عنه قام إليه في مسيره إلى البصرة فقال يا أمير المؤمنين علام تَقَدِّمُنا؟ فقال: على الإصلاح وإطفاء الثائرة، لعل الله شمل هذه الأمة يجمع ويضع حربهم، وقد أجابوا»، قال: «فإن لم يجيبوا؟» قال: «تركناهم ما تركونا»، قال فإن لم يتركونا؟» قال: «دفعناهم عن أنفسنا»، قال: فهل لهم مثل ما عليهم من هذا؟ قال: «نعم». وروي رواية ظاهرة أن عليا قام عند مُنصَرَفه من الشام وخروج الخوارج عليه وإنكارهم التحكيم وإكفارهم معاوية وأهل الشام والبصرة عند خطاب ورأي أشارت عليه به همذان وجارية بن قدامة التميمي (١) ، فقال عند ذلك : «إنا والله ما قاتلنا أهل الشام على ما توهم هؤلاء الضلال من التكفير والفراق في الدين وما قاتلناهم إلا لنردهم إلى الجماعة وأتاكم هذا منهم في الفُرْقة وإنهم لإخواننا في الدين قبلتنا واحدة ورأينا أننا على الحق ودونهم وإني لعلى عهد من رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وأمر أمرني فيه بقتال الباغين والناكثين وإن الرشاد عندي أن يجمعنا الله وإياهم وما لهذا الأمر مثل الرفق عسى الله أن هذه الفرقة إلى ما كانت عليه من الجماعة فما كره الصلح من هؤلاء يجمع الضلال إلا كان يكره الجهاد للعدو ويَضنُّون بأنفسهم عن الحرب ويريدون من الاعتداء على المسلمين لقد كنا مع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وإن القتل بين الآباء والأبناء والإخوان وذوي القرابات فما نزداد على كل مصيبة وكل شدة إلا إيماناً ونصراً للحق وسلمنا الأمر الله تعالى وصبرنا على ألم الجراح ولكنا لما أصبحنا نقاتل إخواننا في الإسلام على ما دخل فيهم من الزيغ والتأويل والشبهة فإذا طمعنا في خَصْلَةٍ يَكُمُ الله بها شعثنا ونزلنا بها إلى التقية بيننا ورغبنا فيها وأمسكنا عما سواها . . . » ثم قطع الكلام . ما قاله علي مفارق لما تعتقد الشيعة والشَّرَاة في أهل البصرة وجميع وصفين من إكفارهم وإخراجهم عن الإيمان فلا عذر لِمُشَع في الخلاف عليه والسرَفِ والإغراق في إكفار المحارب له على التأويل والقاعد عنه، والحال ما وصفناه .
And it has been narrated that al-A'war ibn Aban al-Minqari, who was among the noble companions of Ali (may Allah be pleased with him), stood before him during his march to Basra and said: 'O Commander of the Faithful, on what basis do you lead us?' He replied: 'For reconciliation and extinguishing the spark; perhaps Allah will gather this community and remove their war, and they have responded.' He said: 'If they do not respond?' He said: 'We will leave them as long as they leave us.' He said: 'If they do not leave us?' He said: 'We will defend ourselves against them.' He said: 'Do they have the same obligations as we have in this?' He said: 'Yes.' And it has been narrated in a clear report that Ali, upon his return from Sham and upon the emergence of the Khawarij against him and their denial of arbitration and their declaring Mu'awiyah and the people of Sham and Basra as disbelievers, during a speech and an opinion suggested to him by Hamdan and Jariyah ibn Qudamah al-Tamimi (1), said on that occasion: 'By Allah, we did not fight the people of Sham for what these misguided ones imagine of takfir and separation in religion; we only fought them to bring them back to the community (jama'ah). And this (action) from them (the Khawarij) is due to division. Indeed, they are our brothers in religion; our qiblah is one. We believe we are upon truth and they are not. And I have a covenant from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) and a command he commanded me regarding fighting the aggressors (al-baghin) and the breakers of oaths (al-nakithin). Indeed, the right course in my view is that Allah should gather us and them together. There is nothing like gentleness for this matter; perhaps Allah will (reunite) this faction to what it was before of community. Whoever dislikes reconciliation among these people who gather misguidance is like one who dislikes jihad against the enemy, and they are stingy with themselves regarding war and desire aggression against Muslims. We were with the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) and killing occurred between fathers, sons, brothers, and close relatives, yet we only increased in every calamity and every hardship in faith and support for truth, and we submitted the matter to Allah Almighty and endured the pain of wounds. But when we came to fight our brothers in Islam due to what entered into them of deviation, misinterpretation, and doubt, if we hope for a quality by which Allah may mend our disunity and we descend to truce between us, we desire it and refrain from other than it...' Then he stopped speaking. What Ali said is contrary to what the Shi'a and the Khawarij (shurah) believe regarding the people of Basra and all of Siffin: that they are disbelievers and removed from faith. So there is no excuse for anyone to oppose him in that, and to go to extremes and excess in declaring as disbelievers those who fight him due to interpretation (ta'wil) and those who sit back (from fighting), while the situation is as we have described.
Source pages p. 561

Commentary
This passage is an argument from historical report (riwaya) used by Baqillani to refute the extremist positions of the Shi'a and Khawarij who declared Ali's opponents (e.g., the people of Basra and Siffin) as disbelievers. Baqillani presents two narrations to show Ali's actual stance. First, the story of al-A'war ibn Aban asking Ali about the purpose of his march to Basra. Ali replies that he seeks reconciliation and only fights defensively, and affirms that the opponents have the same duties (i.e., they are Muslims). Second, a longer report where Ali explicitly denies fighting for takfir and declares that his opponents are 'brothers in religion' with the same qiblah, and he only fights them due to their rebellion and misinterpretation. Ali emphasizes that he follows the Prophet's command to fight aggressors and oath-breakers, not to declare them disbelievers. He also laments the division and expresses hope for reconciliation. After this quote, Baqillani concludes that Ali's words are contrary to what the Shi'a and Khawarij believe—namely, that those opponents are disbelievers. Therefore, there is no excuse for anyone to differ with Ali, nor to go to extremes in declaring takfir against those who fought Ali due to interpretation or those who abstained from fighting. The passage thus establishes the correct Sunni stance that such conflicts among Companions do not entail takfir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ali declared his opponents at Siffin and Basra as disbelievers.
Correction: This passage shows Ali explicitly saying he did not fight for takfir and that they are brothers in religion.

Checkpoint
According to the narration cited by Baqillani, why did Ali fight the people of Sham?
A) Because they were disbelievers
B) To bring them back to the community (jama'ah) and stop division
C) To seek revenge for Uthman
D) Because they rejected the caliphate

Key Takeaway
Ali's own words confirm that he did not declare his opponents disbelievers; he fought only to reunite the community, and Baqillani uses this to refute extremism in takfir.
